The Criterion for Opposing Motions
Defines the condition for two motions to be opposites and why ascending/descending meet it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why only ascending and descending motions are considered opposites in classical kalam, and what condition distinguishes them from other motion pairs.

Core Idea
Opposition requires extreme difference of species, not just opposite directions.

In this passage, the author establishes a precise criterion for two motions to be considered opposites (mutadaddan). He argues that mere opposite directions are insufficient; the motions must belong to two distinct species within a genus, with the maximum possible difference. Ascending and descending from the center to the circumference and back meet this condition, while other pairs like rectilinear back-and-forth or curved motions do not. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the author's examples: ascending/descending, rectilinear return motion, and curved motions.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ghāyat al-khilāf' (extreme difference) – it is the key concept.
2
Identify the condition for opposition
The author states that ascending and descending are opposites because they cannot coexist at one time/direction and are two species with extreme difference. What exactly does this mean?
Contrast with rectilinear motion from point A to B and back – that is one species, not two.
3
Understand why curved motions fail the condition
The author argues that between any two points there are infinite arcs, and no curved motion is at the extreme because there is always a more curved one. This shows that extreme difference is not achieved.
The discussion about 'absolute curvature' shows that only specific instances exist, and each has a more different one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الحركتين لأنهما أعني الصاعدة والهابطة أمران وجوديان يمتنع اجتماعهما في زمان واحد من جهة واحدة مع كونهما نوعين من جنس بينهما غاية الخلاف وهذا بخلاف الحركة المستقيمة نقطة المسافة إلى نقطة مع الرجوع عنها إلى الأول لا بطريق الصعود والهبوط فإنهما نوع واحد وبخلاف المستقيمة والمنحنية أو المنحنيتين وإن كانت إحداهما فوق والأخرى تحت فإنهما ليستا على غاية الخلاف لأن بين كل نقطتين قسيا غير متناهية والعظمى أشد انحناء فأشد مخالفة. ولا يجوز أن يعتبر مطلق الانحناء لأنه لا يوجد في الخارج إلا في ضمن معين وكل معين يوجد فما فوقه أشد مخالفة منه
From the two motions, because they, I mean the ascending and descending, are two existent things whose coexistence at one time from one direction is impossible, while they are two species of a genus between which there is extreme difference. This is unlike rectilinear motion from point to point with return to the first not by way of ascending and descending, for they are one species. And unlike rectilinear and curved, or two curved motions, even if one is above and the other below, for they are not at extreme difference because between any two points there are infinite arcs, and the greater one is more curved and thus more different. And it is not permissible to consider absolute curvature because it does not exist externally except in a specific instance, and every specific instance that exists has above it a more different one.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for two motions to be considered opposites (mutadāddān). The author argues that ascending (al-ṣā‘idah) and descending (al-hābiṭah) are opposites because they are two existent things that cannot coexist at the same time and direction, and they are two species (naw‘ān) of a genus (jins) with extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) between them. This is contrasted with rectilinear motion from one point to another and back, which is considered one species because the path is reversible without qualitative difference. Similarly, a straight and a curved motion, or two curved motions, even if one is above and the other below, are not at extreme difference because between any two points there are infinitely many arcs (qusiyyun), with the larger arc being more curved (ashaddu inḥinā’) and thus more different—but never at an extreme. The author then notes that absolute curvature (muṭlaq al-inḥinā’) cannot be considered because it does not exist in external reality except in a specific instance, and any specific instance has a more different one above it. Thus, only ascending and descending from the center to the circumference and back meet the criterion.

Key Terms
تضاد
غاية الخلاف
حركة صاعدة
حركة هابطة
حركة مستقيمة
منحنية
قوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two motions in opposite directions (e.g., up and down) are automatically opposites.
Correction: Opposition requires not just opposite directions but that the motions be two species of a genus with extreme difference. Ascending and descending meet this, but other direction pairs do not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are ascending and descending motions considered opposites?
A) They cannot coexist at the same time and direction.
B) They are two species of a genus with extreme difference between them.
C) Both A and B.
D) They are the only straight-line motions.

Key Takeaway
The condition for two motions to be opposites is that they are two species of a genus with extreme difference and cannot coexist at one time from one direction; only ascending and descending fully meet this condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim two beliefs or actions are incompatible, have you ever considered whether they are truly opposites (extreme difference of species) or merely different in degree or direction? How might that distinction change your approach to resolving conflicts in your life?
?
This lesson shows that absolute concepts (like absolute curvature) have no external reality; only specific instances exist. Where in your own understanding of faith or practice do you rely on abstract absolutes rather than concrete realities? What might you need to re-examine as a result?

Three Conditions for Opposition of Motions
Explores three specific scenarios for opposition of motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three specific scenarios where motion opposition is evaluated, including the role of starting points, endpoints, and the concept of extreme difference.

Core Idea
Opposition of motions requires opposite start and end points, not just opposite directions.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid examines the precise conditions under which two motions are considered opposites. The author identifies three investigations, including motions along an arc, rectilinear motions, and the necessity of both starting and ending points being opposite. The discussion also engages with Ibn Sina's view on water's natural motions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation to get the overall flow. Notice that it lists three distinct investigations.
2
Identify the three investigations
Look for numbered items: 'the first', 'the second', 'the third'. Each presents a scenario related to motion opposition.
3
Examine the criteria for opposition
Pay attention to the condition of extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) and the requirement that both starting points (mabda’) and endpoints (muntaḥā) be opposite.
The examples clarify what counts as a true opposite motion.
4
Analyze Ibn Sina's example
Consider the case of water's natural motions: why are they not opposites? Connect it to the condition of extreme distance.
This illustrates the practical application of the criteria.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن القوس التي تماس محدب الفلك المحيط في غاية الخلاف فالحركة عليها ينبغي أن تكون ضداً للحركة المستقيمة لصدق الحد بجميع شرائطه. الثاني : إن الصاعدة والهابطة المستقيمتين أيضاً قد لا تكونان على غاية الخلاف كالصعود من وجه الأرض إلى النار والهبوط منها إليه لظهور أن الصعود إلى الفلك أشد مخالفة لذلك الهبوط، والهبوط إلى مركز الأرض أشد مخالفة لذلك الصعود . الثالث : إن ظاهر كلامهم هو أن المعتبر في تضاد الحركتين تضاد مبدأيهما وتضاد منتهيهما جميعاً ، فالصعود والهبوط من المركز والمحيط إلى حيز من الهواء مثلا لا يكونان متضادين لاتحاد المنتهى، وكذا الصعود والهبوط منه إلى المحيط والمركز لاتحاد المبدأ، وقد صرح ابن سينا بأنه لا تضاد بين حركتي الماء بالطبع من فوق الهواء ومن تحت الأرض لأنهما ينتهيان إلى نهاية واحدة، ولا يظهر لهذا سبب سوى ما ذكره الإمام : وهو أنهما ليستا على غاية التباعد لأن البعد بين حركة النار وحركة الأرض أكثر من البعد بين الماء من المركز وهبوطه عن المحيط (وعلى هذا لا يتحقق التضاد في صعود الحركات الأينية إلا بين الصعود من المركز إلى المحيط، والهبوط من المحيط) إلى المركز إذ فيما سوى ذلك لا يتحقق ما اعبروه ههنا في من غاية التباعد، وكون ضد الواحد واحداً، وهم مصرحون بأن حركتي الحجر علواً وسفلاً بالقسر والطبع متضادتان
The first: The arc that touches the convexity of the encompassing sphere is at extreme difference, so motion upon it should be opposite to rectilinear motion due to the truth of the definition with all its conditions. The second: The rectilinear ascending and descending also may not be at extreme difference, like ascending from the face of the earth to the fire and descending from it to him, because it is apparent that ascending to the sphere is more different from that descending, and descending to the center of the earth is more different from that ascending. The third: The apparent meaning of their statement is that what is considered in the opposition of two motions is the opposition of their starting points and the opposition of their endpoints together. So ascending and descending from the center and the circumference to a region of air, for example, are not opposites because the endpoint is unified. Likewise, ascending and descending from it to the circumference and the center because the starting point is unified. And Ibn Sina stated that there is no opposition between the two natural motions of water from above the air and from under the earth because they end at one limit. And no cause appears for this except what the Imam mentioned: that they are not at extreme distance because the distance between the motion of fire and the motion of earth is greater than the distance between the motion of water from the center and its descent from the circumference. And based on this, opposition is not realized in local motions except between ascent from the center to the circumference and descent from the circumference to the center, since in other than that what they considered here of extreme distance and that the opposite of one is one is not realized. And they explicitly state that the two motions of a stone upward and downward by coercion and by nature are opposites.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage presents three specific investigations (mawāḍi‘ baḥth) into the conditions for opposition of motions. The first investigation argues that the arc (al-qaws) that touches the convex surface of the encompassing celestial sphere is at the extreme of difference from a straight line, so motion along that arc should be opposite to rectilinear motion, fulfilling all conditions of the definition of opposition. The second investigation shows that even rectilinear ascending and descending are not always at extreme difference. For example, ascending from the earth's surface to the sphere of fire is less different from descending from fire to earth than from other ascents. The third investigation clarifies that the criterion for opposition is the opposition of both the starting point (mabda’) and the endpoint (muntaḥā) together. If either is the same, the motions are not opposite. For instance, ascending from the center to a region of air and descending from the circumference to the same air have the same endpoint, so they are not opposites. Similarly, if the starting point is the same. The passage cites Ibn Sina's view that water's natural motion from above air and from under earth are not opposite because they both end at the same limit (earth's surface). The Imam (al-Imām) explains that the distance between the motions of fire and earth is greater than that between water's two motions, so water's motions are not at extreme distance. Consequently, genuine opposition in local motions only occurs between ascent from center to circumference and descent from circumference to center. The commentators also affirm that a stone's upward and downward motions, whether by force (qasr) or nature (ṭab‘), are opposites.

Key Terms
قوس
محدب الفلك
مبدأ
منتهى
ابن سينا
الإمام
غاية التباعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two motions in opposite directions (e.g., up and down) are always opposites.
Correction: Opposition requires that both the starting points and endpoints be opposite; up and down motions that share an endpoint or starting point are not opposites.

Checkpoint
According to the third investigation, why are ascending and descending motions that share the same endpoint not opposites?
A) Their starting points are opposite.
B) Their endpoints are the same.
C) They are not at extreme difference.
D) They are both natural motions.

Key Takeaway
For two motions to be opposites, their starting points and endpoints must both be opposite; sharing either makes them non-opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your spiritual life, do you sometimes label two actions or states as 'opposites' based only on surface appearances (like up/down), without examining their true starting points (intentions) and endpoints (ultimate goals)? How might this lead to a false sense of opposition that hinders your understanding of yourself before Allah?
?
The passage shows that even experts like Ibn Sina and the Imam carefully analyze the conditions for opposition. When was the last time you subjected your own assumptions about right and wrong, or about good and evil, to such rigorous scrutiny? Are you willing to do so today, even if it challenges your comfortable categories?
?
Consider the motion of a stone: by force upward, by nature downward, they are opposites. In your life, what 'motions' (habits, choices, inclinations) are truly opposites because they start from and end at opposing spiritual realities? Identify one and ask Allah for clarity.

The Imam's Criterion: Opposition of Descriptions
Presents the Imam's refined criterion for motion opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Imam's refined criterion that motion opposition depends on the opposition of the descriptions of being a starting point and an endpoint, not the points themselves.

Core Idea
Motion opposes motion when their starting and ending descriptions are opposites, not the points.

In this fourth investigation, al-Imām refines the criterion for opposition of motions. He argues that it is not the physical points that matter, but their roles as 'start' and 'end'. This subtle shift has important implications for understanding how motions relate. Let's read his exact formulation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the key elements
The passage presents the Imam's view: opposition of motion requires opposition of the starting point and endpoint in terms of their descriptions (being start or end), not the essences themselves.
The essential relation (al-ta‘alluq al-dhātī) is to the descriptions, not the points.
3
Understand the implication
Ask yourself: what would happen if the relation were to the points themselves? The Imam's view prevents certain false oppositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن الإمام قد اعتبر في تضاد الحركة تضاد المبدأ والمنتهى من حيث وصف المبدئية والمنتهية، وذكر أن التعلق الذاتي للحركة لما كان بنفس الوصفين دون الذاتين، إذ لو لم يعرض للنقطتين كونها مبدأ وغاية للحركة لم يكن للحركة تعلق بهما أوجب تضاد الإطلاق تضاد الحركات . فإن قيل : موجب تضاد الحركتين تضاد مبدأيهما، وتضاد منتهيهما لاتضاد المبدأ والمنتهى قلنا معنى الكلام لأن المبدأ والمنتهى لما كانا متضادين كانت الصاعدة والهابطة مبدأهما متضدين لكونهما مبدأ ومنتهى للصاعدة، وكذلك منتهاهما لكونهما مبدأ ومنهى للهابطة فإن قيل : فيلزم التضاد بين كل حركة مستقيمة من نقطة إلى أخرى مع الرجوع عنها إلى الأولى بل المستديرة أيضاً كما إذا تحرك جسم من أول الحمل إلى أول الميزان، ثم رجع عنه إلى أول الحمل بحيث يكون ممر الحركتين على الحمل، والثور والجوزاء والسرطان والأسد والسنبلة ويتحقق البداية والنهاية بالفعل، فلا يندفع بما قيل إن الحركة على التوالي لا تضاد الحركة على خلاف التوالي لأن كلاً منهما تفعل مثل ما تفعل الأخرى لكن في النصفين على التبادل مثلاً المنحدر من السرطان إلى الجدي على التوالي يكون مسافته الأسد والسنبلة
The fourth: The Imam considered in the opposition of motion the opposition of the starting point and the endpoint in terms of the description of being a starting point and being an endpoint. He mentioned that the essential relation of motion is to the two descriptions themselves, not to the two essences, for if the two points were not considered as starting point and goal of the motion, the motion would have no relation to them that would necessitate the opposition of the motions from the opposition of the [two descriptions?]. If it is said: The cause of opposition of the two motions is the opposition of their starting points and the opposition of their endpoints, not the opposition of the starting point and the endpoint [themselves]? We say: The meaning of the statement is that since the starting point and the endpoint are opposites, the ascending and descending [motions] have their starting points as opposites because they are the start and end for the ascending, and similarly their endpoints because they are the start and end for the descending. If it is said: Then it follows that opposition occurs between every rectilinear motion from one point to another and the return from it to the first, and even circular motion, such as when a body moves from the beginning of Aries to the beginning of Libra, then returns from it to the beginning of Aries such that the path of the two motions passes through Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, and Virgo, and the beginning and end are actual. Then it is not refuted by saying that motion in sequential order is not opposite to motion in reverse sequential order because each does the same as the other, but in the two halves alternately. For example, the descender from Cancer to Capricorn in sequential order has its distance through Leo and Virgo...
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth investigation, which elaborates the Imam's (al-Imām) refined criterion for opposition of motions. The Imam considers the opposition of starting point and endpoint in terms of their descriptions (waṣf) as 'being a starting point' (al-mabda’iyyah) and 'being an endpoint' (al-muntahā’iyyah), not the points themselves as essences. The essential relation (al-ta‘alluq al-dhātī) of motion is to these two descriptions; if the points were not qualified as start or goal, there would be no relation necessitating opposition. The author then presents an objection: one might say that the cause of opposition is the opposition of the starting points of the two motions and the opposition of their endpoints, not the opposition of the description of start and end themselves. The answer clarifies that the meaning is: since the start and end points are opposites, the ascending and descending motions have their starting points opposed (because one point is start for the ascending and end for the descending) and similarly their endpoints opposed. A second objection follows: if so, then every rectilinear motion from one point to another and its return should be opposites, and even circular motions like from the beginning of Aries to the beginning of Libra and back, with a real path through the zodiac signs. The objector claims this cannot be refuted by saying sequential order and reverse sequential order are not opposites because each motion does the same thing but in alternate halves. The example of the descender from Cancer to Capricorn is given, but the text breaks off at the page end, leaving the answer incomplete.

Key Terms

المبدئية
المنتهية
التعلق الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any two motions between different points are opposite.
Correction: The Imam specifies that only motions whose start and end point descriptions are opposite (i.e., one is start for one motion and end for the other, etc.) are opposite.

Checkpoint
According to the Imam, what is the essential relation of motion that determines opposition?
A) The points themselves as essences.
B) The descriptions of being a starting point and an endpoint.
C) The extreme difference in speed.
D) The opposition of the moving bodies.

Key Takeaway
The Imam's criterion for motion opposition focuses on the descriptions of start and end, not the points themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate your own actions, do you focus on the outward results (the points) or the underlying intentions (the descriptions)? How does the Imam's criterion challenge you to rethink what truly makes your deeds praiseworthy or blameworthy? Consider one action today where you can shift your focus from outcome to the 'description' of your intention.
?
In your relationships, do you judge conflicts by the superficial differences (different points) or by the deeper opposing descriptions? Reflect on a recent disagreement: was the opposition truly in the descriptions (e.g., one seeking connection, the other distance) or just in circumstances? How would applying the Imam's criterion change your response?
?
The Imam says motion essentially relates to descriptions, not essences. What 'descriptions' are you attached to in your spiritual life—titles, roles, outward practices—without attending to the essential relation to Allah? What is one description you can purify this week to align with the true purpose of your actions?

Objections to the Imam's Criterion
Raises and answers objections to the Imam's criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the Imam's criterion and its answer, and see the beginning of a second objection about rectilinear and circular motions.

Core Idea
Objections test the criterion: must a return motion always be opposite?

After presenting the Imam's criterion, the text raises two objections. The first challenges whether the cause is opposition of start points or descriptions. The second considers implications for rectilinear and circular motions. Let's examine these objections and the partial answer provided.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the first objection (فإن قيل...) and the answer (قلنا...). Notice the structure: a challenge followed by a clarification.
2
Parse the second objection
The second objection begins with 'فإن قيل: فيلزم التضاد بين كل حركة مستقيمة...' It argues that all rectilinear motions and even circular motions would be opposite. The answer is cut off.
3
Reflect on the incomplete answer
Think about how one might answer the second objection based on the Imam's criterion. What additional condition might be needed?

Speed and Slowness as Relational Qualities of Motion
The author defines speed and slowness as relational qualities of motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that speed and slowness are relational qualities of motion, not intrinsic properties, and how they are defined through the comparison of distance and time.

Core Idea
Speed and slowness are relational, not absolute.

In this passage, the author examines the necessary concomitants of motion, focusing on the qualities of speed and slowness. He explains that these are not inherent to motion but arise from a comparative assessment of distance and time. Understanding this relational nature is crucial for grasping how motion is quantified in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the definitions given.
The passage uses a logical progression from definition to explanation.
2
Identify the definitions of speed and slowness
Find how the author defines speed (سرعة) and slowness (بطء) in terms of intensity and weakness, and as relational.
They are defined as qualities capable of intensity and weakness, varying by relation.
3
Understand the role of distance and time
Notice how the author explains that speed and slowness are expressed only through what follows from them: crossing a longer distance in equal time, etc.
This shows their relational nature.
4
Consider the relativity of judgments
Reflect on the statement that a single motion can be fast relative to one thing and slow relative to another, depending on the comparison.
This underscores that speed and slowness are not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس: لوازم الحركة الكيفية. قال: من لوازم الحركة كيفية قابلة للشدة والضعف مختلفة بحسب الإضافة يسمى باعتبار الشدة سرعة، وباعتبار الضعف بطئاً. لا بد للحركة من زمان ومن امتداد في الأين أو المقادير أو الكيفيات أو الأوضاع ولا بأس بتسميته مسافة وإن كان الاسم بإطلاقه لما في الأين وهما أعني الزمان والمسافة يقبلان القسمة فإذا فرضنا قطع مسافة في زمان، فقد يقطع تلك المسافة في زمان أقل، أو يقطع في ذلك الزمان مسافة أقل مع أن حقيقة الحركة بحالها فلا محالة يكون ذلك بصفة في الحركة يشتد فيقطع المسافة الأطول وتسمى سرعة، وتضعف فتقطع المسافة الأقصر وتسمى بطئاً، ولا تقدر على التعبير عنهما إلا بما يلزمهما من قطع المسافة الأطول في زمان مساو أو المسافة المساوية في زمان أقل، وقطع المسافة الأقصر في زمان مساو أو المسافة المساوية في زمان أكثر، ويختلفان بحسب الاعتبار فتكون الحركة الواحدة سريعة بالنسبة إلى ما يقطع مسافتها في زمان أكثر أو يقطع في زمانها مسافة أقل وبطيئة بالنسبة إلى عكس ذلك.
The fifth section: On the necessary concomitants of qualitative motion. He said: Among the necessary concomitants of motion is a quality capable of intensity and weakness, varying according to relation. It is called, with respect to intensity, speed, and with respect to weakness, slowness. Motion must have time and extension in place, or quantities, or qualities, or positions; and there is no harm in calling it distance, even if the name in its absolute sense is for what is in place. Both of them, namely time and distance, admit division. So if we suppose crossing a distance in a time, it may be that one crosses that distance in a shorter time, or crosses a shorter distance in that same time, while the reality of motion remains as it is. So necessarily that is due to a quality in motion: it intensifies, so it crosses the longer distance, and is called speed; and it weakens, so it crosses the shorter distance, and is called slowness. And one cannot express them except through what necessarily follows them: crossing the longer distance in an equal time, or crossing an equal distance in a shorter time; and crossing the shorter distance in an equal time, or crossing an equal distance in a longer time. And they differ according to consideration, so a single motion is fast relative to that which crosses its distance in more time, or crosses in its time a shorter distance; and slow relative to the opposite.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage, from the fifth section on the necessary concomitants of motion, defines speed and slowness. The author states that motion has a necessary concomitant: a quality that admits intensity and weakness, and varies by relation. This quality is called speed when intense, and slowness when weak. He then explains that motion requires time and extension – either in place (al-ayn), or quantities, or qualities, or positions; this extension can be called distance (masafa), though the name is primarily for place. Both time and distance are divisible. The author then describes relative judgment: if one crosses a distance in a time, one might cross that same distance in less time, or cross a shorter distance in the same time, while motion's reality remains unchanged. This difference is due to a quality in motion: intensification leads to crossing a longer distance (speed), weakening leads to crossing a shorter distance (slowness). These can only be expressed by their relational concomitants, e.g., crossing a longer distance in equal time, or equal distance in less time, etc. Finally, the same motion can be considered fast relative to one that takes more time or covers less distance, and slow relative to the opposite. Thus, speed and slowness are relational qualities.

Key Terms

الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By the distance crossed and time taken
B) By their intensity and weakness alone
C) By the division of the moving object
D) By the nature of place

Key Takeaway
Speed and slowness are relational qualities expressed through the comparison of distance and time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that speed and slowness are relational, not absolute. In your spiritual life, what 'motion' (action or habit) have you labeled as 'slow' in your progress? Now ask yourself: slow compared to what? To someone else? To an unrealistic ideal? Are you using the wrong standard?
?
Consider a single action you performed today—like praying or reading Quran. Relative to someone who does less, it may be 'fast'; relative to someone who does more, it may be 'slow.' Which comparison do you instinctively make? And which one does Allah want you to make?
?

Does that humble you? Will you let it drive you to increase, or will you become complacent? Choose your standard wisely.

The Cause of Slowness: Internal and External Resistance
Al-Taftazani states the causes of slowness with internal and external resistance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how slowness in motion is caused by two types of resistance: internal resistance for forced and voluntary motion, and external resistance for all motions, including natural motion.

Core Idea
Slowness comes from resistance – either from within the moving body or from the medium.

Al-Taftazani explains that slowness (but') is not a random property but has specific causes. He distinguishes between two types of resistance: internal, which affects forced and voluntary motion, and external, which affects all motions including natural motion. This passage clarifies the physical principles underlying motion in the kalam tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's own formulation.
The passage starts with 'قال' indicating a direct quote from the author.
2
Identify the two types of resistance
Notice the two categories: internal resistance (mu'aawiga daakhiliyya) and external resistance (mu'aawiga khaarijiyya). Internal is from the moving body itself; external is from the medium.
3
Understand which motions each applies to
Internal resistance applies to forced and voluntary motion, but not natural motion. External resistance applies to all motions (natural, forced, voluntary). Find examples for each in the passage.
The reason internal resistance excludes natural motion is explained: something cannot both necessitate and prevent its own motion.
4
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself why natural motion cannot have internal resistance. The passage states that it is impossible for a thing to both necessitate and prevent something. Discuss this logical principle.
This connects to the learning objective about causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وسبب البطء المعاوقة الداخلية في غير الحركة الطبيعية والخارجية في الكل.
يعني أن المعاوقة التي تكون من نفس المتحرك كثقل الجسم يصلح سبباً لبطء الحركة القسرية كما في الحجر المرمى إلى فوق والإرادية كما في صعود الإنسان الجبل، لا الطبيعية لامتناع أن يكون الشيء مقتضياً لأمر، ومانعاً عنه، والمعاوقة التي تكون من الخارج كغلظ قوام ما يتحرك فيه يصلح سبباً لبطء الحركة الطبيعية كنزول الحجر في الماء والقسرية والإرادية كحوكة السهم والإنسان فيه
He said: And the cause of slowness is internal resistance in non-natural motion, and external resistance in all. Meaning that the resistance that comes from the moving body itself, like the weight of the body, is a valid cause for the slowness of forced motion, as in a stone thrown upwards, and voluntary motion, as in a person climbing a mountain, not natural motion, because it is impossible that a thing should necessitate something and also prevent it. And the resistance that comes from outside, like the thickness of the medium in which motion occurs, is a valid cause for the slowness of natural motion, as in a stone falling in water, and forced and voluntary motions, like the difficulty of an arrow or a person in it.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage discusses the cause of slowness (sabab al-but'). It states that the cause is internal resistance (mu'aawiga daakhiliyya) in non-natural motion (ghayr al-haraka al-tabi'iyya) and external resistance (khaarijiyya) in all motions. Then an explanation (ya'ni) clarifies: internal resistance is from the moving body itself, like the weight of a body in forced motion (e.g., a stone thrown upward) or voluntary motion (e.g., climbing a mountain); not in natural motion because something cannot both necessitate and prevent its own effect. External resistance is from the medium, like the thickness of the substance in which motion occurs, causing slowness in natural motion (e.g., stone falling in water) as well as forced and voluntary motions (e.g., arrow or person moving through viscous medium).

Key Terms
البطء
المعاوقة
الداخلية
الخارجية
الحركة الطبيعية
الحركة القسرية
الحركة الإرادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Slowness is a separate motion or property independent of resistance.
Correction: Slowness is caused by resistance; it is not a separate motion but a quality determined by the amount of resistance.

Checkpoint
In which type of motion does internal resistance NOT play a role in causing slowness?
A) Forced motion.
B) Voluntary motion.
C) Natural motion.
D) Both forced and voluntary.

Key Takeaway
Slowness is caused by internal resistance in forced and voluntary motion, and by external resistance in all motions, with the exception that internal resistance does not affect natural motion due to logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Are you allowing external resistance—like bad company or a toxic environment—to slow your obedience? How will you change your surroundings this week?
?
If your soul has a natural inclination towards good, what internal resistance are you placing against it? How can you align your inner desires with your outer actions?

Refutation of the Three Proofs Against Tahlil al-Sakanat
Al-Taftazani refutes each of the three proofs against the analysis of motion into rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes three arguments against the analysis of motion into rests by challenging their assumptions about logical necessity, causality, and perception.

Core Idea
The refutations show that the apparent continuity of motion is due to habit, divine creation, and perceptual fusion.

After presenting the three proofs against tahliil al-sakanat, al-Taftazani now provides their refutations. Each refutation challenges a different assumption: the first concerns logical necessity, the second concerns causality, and the third concerns perception. This lesson examines how each response undermines the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutations
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the three distinct refutations.
Each refutation begins with 'وال' (and the).
2
Analyze the first refutation
The first refutation says the coexistence of two motions is 'aadi (habitual) not 'aqli (rational). Understand why this distinction matters.
If it is habitual, then it could be otherwise, so the proof fails.
3
Analyze the second refutation
The second refutation attributes motion solely to God's creation, denying any influence from created powers.
This refutes the proof's assumption that powers cause motion.
4
Analyze the third refutation
The third refutation explains that motions and rests intermingle, and motions unify the rests perceptually.
This accounts for the appearance of continuous motion.
5
Consider the overall strategy
Notice that each refutation addresses a different aspect: logical necessity, causation, and perception.
Together they form a comprehensive response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الأول : بأن تلازم الحركتين عادي لا عقلي، فلا يمتنع الافتراق .
والثاني : بأن الحركات بمحض خلق الله تعالى من غير تأثير للقوى .
والثالث : بأن امتزجت الحركات والسكنات بحيث لا يتميز عند بأزمنتهما، والحركات لكونها وجودية متحدة بين السكنات، وإن كانت متكثرة .
He refuted the first: that the coexistence of two motions is habitual, not rational, so separation is not impossible. And the second: that motions are by the mere creation of God, may He be exalted, without any influence of powers. And the third: that motions and rests intermingle such that they are not distinguished at [؟] in their times, and motions, because they are existent, unify the rests, even if they are multiple.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage provides answers to the three proofs. For the first proof, it says the coexistence of two motions is habitual (aadi), not rational (aqli), so separation is not impossible. For the second proof, it says motions are solely by the creation of God (bi-mahd khalq Allah) without any influence of powers (quwa). For the third proof, it says motions and rests are so intermingled that they cannot be distinguished in their times, and motions, being existent, unify the rests even if the rests are multiple. That is, the perception of continuous motion is due to the fusion of motions and rests.

Key Terms
عادي
عقلي
محض خلق الله
امتزجت السكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these refutations deny the arguments entirely, but they actually show the arguments rely on assumptions that can be contested.
Correction: The refutations do not deny the phenomena; they reinterpret them by challenging the assumptions of logical necessity, causal power, and perceptual distinctness.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation of the first proof respond to the claim that two motions cannot coexist?
A) It denies the observation.
B) It says the coexistence is by habit, not logical necessity.
C) It says the motions are actually one.
D) It invokes divine intervention.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani refutes each proof by challenging its underlying assumption: habit vs. necessity, divine causation, and perceptual fusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a continuous motion in your daily life, do you take it as a sign of God's direct creation or as a mere habit of nature? How would your awareness of God's constant action change your gratitude?
?
Al-Taftazani argues that motions are purely from God, not from any power. Can you identify an area of your life where you rely on your own 'power' or 'ability' rather than acknowledging that God creates each action? What would change if you consciously attributed every movement to Him?
?
The third refutation says that motions unify rests, making you perceive continuity. In your spiritual life, are there 'rests'—periods of stillness or inactivity—that you ignore, thinking they are unimportant? How might these rests actually be part of a greater motion that God is weaving in your journey?

First Argument: Concomitance of Motions and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents the first argument against motion decomposing into rests and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against the decomposition of motion into rests, which claims that it would break the concomitance of two motions, and its refutation based on the distinction between rational and customary concomitance.

Core Idea
Concomitance is customary, not rational, so motion can decompose into rests.

The first argument against the decomposition of motion into rests relies on the observation that certain motions are normally concomitant, like the sun and its shadow. The argument claims that if motion were composed of rests, the rests would vary and break the concomitance. The refutation clarifies that this concomitance is merely customary, not rationally necessary, so it can be broken.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Read the first part of the Arabic passage that presents the argument from concomitance of motions. Then read the translation. Notice the examples: motion of the sun and shadow, and motion of the two ends of a millstone.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument is: if motion decomposes into rests, then the rests would be different, breaking the usual concomitance. Since the concomitance is true, the premise must be false.
3
Read the refutation
The refutation states that the concomitance is customary, not rational. This means it is not a logical necessity but a pattern that God could break.
Understand the distinction between ʿādī (customary) and ʿaqlī (rational).
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does this matter for the atomistic view of motion? The refutation allows for the possibility that motion is composed of discrete rests, as per the Ashʿarī occasionalist worldview.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وسبب البطء المعاوقة الداخلية في غير الحركة الطبيعية والخارجية في الكل، ولا تحلل السكنات لوجوه : الأول : أنه يستلزم امتناع تلازم الحركتين مع تفاوت المسافتين لاستلزامه تفاوت السكنات المنافي لتلازم الحركات واللازم منتف كما في حركة الشمس، وما يتخيل من حركة الظل، وفي حركة طرفي الرحى ونحو ذلك . الثاني : إن انتفاء الحركة مع تحقق المقتضى وعدم المانع ضروري البطلان . الثالث: إن فضل سكنات الفرس الشديد العدو حينئذ على حركاته، كفضل حركات الفلك الأعظم عليها، فيلزم أن يرى ساكناً على الدوام، لكون الحركات مغمورة أو في زمان هو أضعاف آلاف زمان الحركة لا أقل للقطع بأن الجسم حال السكون يرى ساكناً، وإن كان السكون عدمياً . ورد الأول : بأن تلازم الحركتين عادي لا عقلي، فلا يمتنع الافتراق . والثاني : بأن الحركات بمحض خلق الله تعالى من غير تأثير للقوى . الثالث : بأن امتزجت الحركات والسكنات بحيث لا يتميز عند بأزمنتهما، والحركات لكونها وجودية متحدة بين السكنات ، وإن كانت متكثرة) .
He said: The cause of slowness is internal resistance in non-natural motion and external resistance in all, and they do not analyze into rests for reasons: First: That it would necessitate the impossibility of the concomitance of two motions with difference in distances because it would necessitate difference in rests which is incompatible with the concomitance of motions, and the consequent is false as in the motion of the sun and what is imagined of the motion of the shadow, and in the motion of the two ends of the millstone and the like. Second: That the absence of motion with the presence of the requirer and the absence of an obstacle is necessarily false. Third: That the excess of rests of the extremely fast horse over its motions is like the excess of the motions of the outermost sphere over them, so it would follow that it is seen as constantly at rest because the motions are submerged or in a time that is thousands of times the time of motion, at least, since it is certain that a body in a state of rest is seen as at rest, even if rest is non-existent. The first was answered: that the concomitance of the two motions is customary not rational, so separation is not impossible. The second: that motions are solely by the creation of God Most High without any effect of the forces. The third: that the motions and rests mix such that they are not distinguished by their times, and motions because they are existent are unified among the rests even if they are multiple.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments against the notion that motion is composed of discrete moments of rest (sakanāt), followed by their refutations. The first argument claims that if motion were composed of rests, two motions that are concomitant (e.g., the motion of the sun and the apparent motion of the shadow) would require equal rests, but they differ, so the premise is false. The refutation distinguishes between rational and customary concomitance: the concomitance is not rational but merely customary, so separation is possible. The second argument states that if motion fails to occur when its requisite (like a force) is present and no obstacle exists, that is impossible. The refutation asserts that motion is created directly by God, without any inherent force, so the principle of 'no effect without cause' does not apply in the same way. The third argument uses the example of a fast horse: if its motion were composed of many rests, the rests would overpower the motions, making it appear stationary. The refutation explains that motions and rests intermingle so finely that they cannot be distinguished by time; the motions, being existent, unify the rests. Thus, the three objections are answered.

Key Terms
سكنات
تلازم
عادي
عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the concomitance of motions is a rational necessity (like logical entailment).
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that it is merely customary, meaning it is a habitual pattern that could be otherwise.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the first argument, why is it not impossible for motion to decompose into rests?
A) Because the two motions are not actually concomitant.
B) Because the concomitance is customary, not rational.
C) Because rests are not real.
D) Because distances are irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The first argument fails because the concomitance of motions is only customary, not a rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the regularity of nature—like the sun causing shadows—do you truly see it as a sign of God's power and custom, or do you secretly assume it is a necessary law independent of God? How does this lesson's refutation of the argument challenge your assumption?
?
If the concommitance of motions is merely customary, what does that imply about the reliability of your daily observations? How does this affect your trust in Allah's consistency?
?
Reflect on a time when you assumed that a natural cause necessarily produced a certain effect. How does this lesson's distinction between customary and rational necessity change how you view that event?

Second Argument: Absence of Motion Despite Requirer and No Obstacle
Taftazani presents the second argument and its refutation based on divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against the decomposition of motion into rests, which relies on the principle that if all conditions are present, motion must occur, and its refutation that motion is directly created by God.

Core Idea
Motion is created by God, not caused by inherent forces.

The second argument assumes that if a requirer (like a force) is present and no obstacle exists, then motion must appear. The refutation rejects this by asserting that motion is solely by God's creation, without any effect from the forces. This reflects the Ashʿarī occasionalist view that God directly creates all events.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic for the second argument and its translation. Understand the claim: absence of motion under the conditions is necessarily false.
2
Understand the underlying assumption
The argument presumes a natural causal connection between force and motion. The refutation denies this causal efficacy.
This is a key difference between Ashʿarī and other Islamic theological schools.
3
Read the refutation
The refutation states that motions are created by God alone. Forces do not produce motion.
Note the term 'khalq' (creation) used.
4
Reflect on the worldview
This refutation is consistent with occasionalism, where God is the sole creator of all events, including motion.

Source Text

This passage presents three arguments against the notion that motion is composed of discrete moments of rest (sakanāt), followed by their refutations. The first argument claims that if motion were composed of rests, two motions that are concomitant (e.g., the motion of the sun and the apparent motion of the shadow) would require equal rests, but they differ, so the premise is false. Third Argument: The Fast Horse and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents the third argument based on perception and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against the decomposition of motion into rests, based on perceptual evidence, and its refutation that motions and rests mix indistinguishably.

Core Idea
Motions and rests intermingle so finely that perception cannot distinguish them.

The third argument uses the example of a fast horse: if its motion were composed of many rests, the rests would outnumber the motions, making the horse appear still. The refutation explains that motions and rests blend together so that they cannot be separated in perception, and motions, being existent, unify the rests.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the Arabic for the third argument and its translation. Note the comparison: the excess of rests in a fast horse's motion is compared to the motions of the outermost sphere.
2
Understand the perceptual claim
The argument says that if rests dominate, the horse should appear stationary. Since it doesn't, the decomposition is false.
This relies on the principle that bodies at rest are perceived as still.
3
Read the refutation
The refutation states that motions and rests mix so that they are not distinguished by their times. Motions, being existent, unify the rests.
The refutation suggests that the combination is too fine for perception.
4
Reflect on the metaphysical implications
This refutation allows for a world of discrete atoms of time and motion, where the whole appears continuous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وسبب البطء المعاوقة الداخلية في غير الحركة الطبيعية والخارجية في الكل، ولا تحلل السكنات لوجوه : الأول : أنه يستلزم امتناع تلازم الحركتين مع تفاوت المسافتين لاستلزامه تفاوت السكنات المنافي لتلازم الحركات واللازم منتف كما في حركة الشمس، وما يتخيل من حركة الظل، وفي حركة طرفي الرحى ونحو ذلك . الثاني : إن انتفاء الحركة مع تحقق المقتضى وعدم المانع ضروري البطلان . الثالث: إن فضل سكنات الفرس الشديد العدو حينئذ على حركاته، كفضل حركات الفلك الأعظم عليها، فيلزم أن يرى ساكناً على الدوام، لكون الحركات مغمورة أو في زمان هو أضعاف آلاف زمان الحركة لا أقل للقطع بأن الجسم حال السكون يرى ساكناً، وإن كان السكون عدمياً . ورد الأول : بأن تلازم الحركتين عادي لا عقلي، فلا يمتنع الافتراق . والثاني : بأن الحركات بمحض خلق الله تعالى من غير تأثير للقوى . الثالث : بأن امتزجت الحركات والسكنات بحيث لا يتميز عند بأزمنتهما، والحركات لكونها وجودية متحدة بين السكنات ، وإن كانت متكثرة) .
He said: The cause of slowness is internal resistance in non-natural motion and external resistance in all, and they do not analyze into rests for reasons: First: That it would necessitate the impossibility of the concomitance of two motions with difference in distances because it would necessitate difference in rests which is incompatible with the concomitance of motions, and the consequent is false as in the motion of the sun and what is imagined of the motion of the shadow, and in the motion of the two ends of the millstone and the like. Second: That the absence of motion with the presence of the requirer and the absence of an obstacle is necessarily false. Third: That the excess of rests of the extremely fast horse over its motions is like the excess of the motions of the outermost sphere over them, so it would follow that it is seen as constantly at rest because the motions are submerged or in a time that is thousands of times the time of motion, at least, since it is certain that a body in a state of rest is seen as at rest, even if rest is non-existent. The first was answered: that the concomitance of the two motions is customary not rational, so separation is not impossible. The second: that motions are solely by the creation of God Most High without any effect of the forces. The third: that the motions and rests mix such that they are not distinguished by their times, and motions because they are existent are unified among the rests even if they are multiple.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments against the notion that motion is composed of discrete moments of rest (sakanāt), followed by their refutations. The first argument claims that if motion were composed of rests, two motions that are concomitant (e.g., the motion of the sun and the apparent motion of the shadow) would require equal rests, but they differ, so the premise is false. The refutation distinguishes between rational and customary concomitance: the concomitance is not rational but merely customary, so separation is possible. The second argument states that if motion fails to occur when its requisite (like a force) is present and no obstacle exists, that is impossible. The refutation asserts that motion is created directly by God, without any inherent force, so the principle of 'no effect without cause' does not apply in the same way. The third argument uses the example of a fast horse: if its motion were composed of many rests, the rests would overpower the motions, making it appear stationary. The refutation explains that motions and rests intermingle so finely that they cannot be distinguished by time; the motions, being existent, unify the rests. Thus, the three objections are answered.

Key Terms
سكنات
حركات
أزمنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a body has many rests, it should appear motionless.
Correction: The refutation says that when motions and rests are intermingled finely, the motions dominate perception and the body appears moving.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the third argument, why does a fast horse not appear stationary?
A) Because rests are not real and are not perceived.
B) Because motions and rests mix so that they are not distinguished by their times, and motions unify the rests.
C) Because the horse's motion is not actually composed of rests.
D) Because the horse is not moving fast enough.

Key Takeaway
The third argument fails because the intermingling of motions and rests prevents perceptual detection of the rests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perceive something as continuous and smooth—like a horse galloping—are you content to accept your senses as completely accurate, or do you admit that there may be a hidden reality you cannot detect? What does that humility demand of you today?
?
The atomists argued that rest dominates motion, yet the horse appears to move. In your spiritual life, what 'rests' (or hidden moments of stillness) might you be ignoring that actually outnumber your 'motions'? How could acknowledging them change your practice?
?
This argument hinges on perception being deceived by intermingled states. In your own heart, are there areas where you trust your immediate perception of yourself (e.g., thinking you are spiritually stagnant or active) while the reality before Allah is far more complex? Will you commit to examining your soul with deeper honesty?

Resistance, Slowness, and Two Schools' Explanations
Taftazani explains types of resistance and two schools' views on slowness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of resistance that cause slowness of motion, and how philosophers and theologians explain the underlying mechanism.

Core Idea
Both schools agree on causes; they differ on the mechanism: inclination vs. rests.

Taftazani distinguishes between internal and external resistance, showing how each affects different types of motion. He then presents the contrasting ontological explanations of the philosophers and theologians, highlighting a key difference in their view of motion and rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the two types of resistance and the two schools' views.
Pay attention to the distinction between internal and external resistance.
2
Identify the three causes of slowness
The passage mentions internal resistance, external resistance, and even the will itself as causes. List them.
Notice that natural motion cannot have internal resistance as a cause.
3
Contrast the two schools' mechanisms
Compare the philosophers' explanation (weakness of inclination) with the theologians' (increase of intervening rests). Understand the difference in their ontological frameworks.
This contrast is central to understanding Islamic cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني أن المعاوقة التي تكون من نفس المتحرك كثقل الجسم يصلح سبباً لبطء الحركة القسرية كما في الحجر المرمى إلى فوق والإرادية كما في صعود الإنسان الجبل، لا الطبيعية لامتناع أن يكون الشيء مقتضياً لأمر، ومانعاً عنه، والمعاوقة التي تكون من الخارج كغلظ قوام ما يتحرك فيه يصلح سبباً لبطء الحركة الطبيعية كنزول الحجر في الماء والقسرية والإرادية كحوكة السهم والإنسان فيه، وقد يكون السبب في بطئها نفس الإرادة كما في رمي الحجر وتحريك اليد برفق ولا خفاء في سببية هذه الأمور في الجملة. لكن عند الفلاسفة من جهة أنها تصير سبباً لضعف الميل الذي هو العلة القريبة للحركة، فيضعف المعلول. وعند المتكلمين من جهة أنه يكثر حينئذ تخلل السكنات التي لا تخلو الحركة عن ثبوتها، وتختلف بالسرعة والبطء بحسب قلتها وكثرتها.
Meaning that the resistance that comes from the mover itself, like the heaviness of the body, is a valid cause for slowness of forced motion, as in a stone thrown upward, and voluntary motion, as in a person climbing a mountain, not natural motion because it is impossible for a thing to require something and be an obstacle to it. And the resistance that comes from outside, like the thickness of the medium in which it moves, is a valid cause for slowness of natural motion like a stone descending in water, and forced and voluntary like the roughness of the arrow and the person in it. And sometimes the cause of its slowness is the will itself, as in throwing a stone and moving the hand gently. There is no hiddenness in the causality of these things in general. But according to the philosophers, it is because they become a cause for the weakness of the inclination (al-mayl) which is the proximate cause of motion, so the effect weakens. And according to the theologians (mutakallimun), it is because then the intervening moments of rest (sakanāt) become numerous, and motion is not free of their existence, and speed and slowness vary according to their fewness and numerousness.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage explains the different types of resistance that cause slowness of motion, and contrasts the philosophers' and theologians' explanations. It first defines internal resistance (like weight) as a cause for slowness in forced and voluntary motion, but not in natural motion, because a thing cannot both require and obstruct its own motion. External resistance (like a thick medium) can affect all types. It notes that even the will itself can be a cause. Both schools accept these as general causes, but they differ on the underlying mechanism: philosophers attribute slowness to the weakening of the inclination (mayl), which is the proximate cause of motion; theologians (mutakallimun) attribute it to an increase in the number of intervening rests (sakanāt). The speed or slowness varies with the fewness or numerousness of these rests. This highlights the fundamental difference between the two schools on the nature of motion and rest.

Key Terms
معاوقة (muʿāwaqa)
ميل (mayl)
علة قريبة (ʿilla qarība)
تخلل (takhallul)
سكنات (sakanāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the two schools disagree on what causes slowness (e.g., one says weight, the other says medium).
Correction: Both schools agree on the observable causes (internal/external resistance). Their disagreement is on the underlying ontological mechanism: weakness of inclination vs. increase of intervening rests.

Checkpoint
According to the theologians (mutakallimun), what is the cause of slowness in motion?
A) Weakening of inclination (mayl).
B) Increase in intervening moments of rest (sakanāt).
C) Thickness of the medium.
D) Internal resistance of the body.

Key Takeaway
Philosophers explain slowness via weakened inclination; theologians explain it via increased intervening rests—both accept the same observable causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the 'resistance' in your own spiritual journey—whether internal like laziness or external like distractions. Are you more focused on weakening the 'inclination' (desire for good) or on reducing the 'rests' (procrastination)? What one concrete step will you take today to remove that resistance?
?

?
If motion cannot be free of rests, then slow spiritual progress is not about avoiding rests but about managing them. What 'rest' have you allowed too many of this week, and what specific 'resistance' made it easier to stop?

Philosophers' Objections to Intervening Rests and Theologians' Initial Response
Philosophers give two objections; theologians start to answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' two objections to the intervening rests explanation of slowness and the beginning of the theologians' response based on denying rational concomitance.

Core Idea
Theologians deny that motion concomitance is rationally necessary, only customary.

The philosophers challenge the theologians' account of slowness as caused by intervening rests. They argue that if rests cause slowness, then two concomitant motions over the same time but different distances would behave inconsistently, breaking the observed concomitance. The theologians begin their response by rejecting the assumption that the concomitance is rationally necessary, opening the door to a different kind of causality grounded in divine custom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage lists two objections and then an answer that is cut off.
2
Identify the two objections
The passage gives two arguments from the philosophers. Find each one and understand how they try to show that rests would break motion concomitance.
The first objection uses a simple case of same time, different distances. The second uses a more complex logical definition of concomitance.
3
Study the examples for the second objection
The philosophers give examples: the sun and shadows, the millstone ends, and the compass branches. Notice how these illustrate that motions can be concomitant even with different speeds.
The shadow example is clarified as an optical effect, not real motion.
4
Examine the theologians' response
The response begins by denying that the concomitance is rationally necessary. Understand what that means for the debate.
The answer is incomplete in this passage, so you only see the opening move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفلاسفة نفوا ذلك بوجوه : الأول : أنه لو كان البطء لتخلل السكنات لامتنع تلازم حركتين مع إتحاد الزمان واختلاف المسافة بالطول والقصر، لأن الحركة التي في المسافة الأقصر تكون أبطأ ضرورة اتحاد الزمان، واختلاف المسافة فيكون تخلل السكنات فيها أكثر، فيصدق أنه قد لا يتحرك . الثاني : عند تحرك الأول فلا يصدق أنه كلما تحرك الثاني تحرك الأول وبالعكس على ما هو معنى التلازم هذا خلف لكن اللازم باطل لتحقق التلازم مع تفاوت المسافة في صور كثيرة كحركة الشمس مع ما يتخيل من حركة ظلال الأشخاص وإنما قلنا يتخيل لأن الظل عرض لا حركة له بل إنما يطرأ عليه الضوء الأول، أعني الضوء الحاصل من مقابلة جرم الشمس فيرى كأنه يتحرك إلى الانتقاص، أو يزول الضوء الأول فيحدث الظل شيئاً فشيئاً فيرى كأنه متحرك إلى الازدياد، وكحركة طرفي الرحى أعني الدائرة العظيمة والصغيرة، وكحركتي الشعبتين الخارجة والمتوسطة من الفرجار ذي الشعب الثلاث عند رسمها الدائرة العظيمة والصغيرة . وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم تلازم الحركتين بمعنى امتناع الانفكاك عقلاً، وإنما
And the philosophers denied that with reasons: First: That if slowness were due to intervening rests, it would be impossible for two motions to be concomitant while the time is one and the distance differs in length and shortness, because the motion in the shorter distance would be slower necessarily due to the unity of time and difference of distance, so the intervention of rests in it would be more, and it would be true that it might not move. Second: When the first moves, it is not true that whenever the second moves the first moves and vice versa according to what is the meaning of concomitance; this is a contradiction, but the consequent is false because concomitance is realized with difference in distance in many cases like the motion of the sun with what is imagined of the motion of the shadows of persons—and we only say 'imagined' because the shadow is an accident (a'rad) having no motion, rather the first light, meaning the light resulting from the facing of the solar body, occurs to it, so it is seen as if it moves toward decrease, or the first light disappears and the shadow arises little by little so it is seen as if it moves toward increase—and like the motion of the two ends of the millstone, meaning the large and small circles, and like the motions of the outer and middle branches of the compass with three branches when drawing the large and small circles. And it was answered: We do not accept the concomitance of the two motions in the sense of the impossibility of separation rationally, but rather...
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' two main objections against the theologians' explanation of slowness by intervening rests, and the beginning of the theologians' response. The first objection argues that if slowness were due to many rests, then two motions that are concomitant over the same time but different distances would require that the slower motion (shorter distance) have more rests, which would imply that it sometimes does not move, contradicting the observed concomitance. The second objection uses a similar argument about the logical relationship between two concomitant motions: if the first moves, it does not imply that the second always moves and vice versa, but if slowness were due to rests, this would be violated. To illustrate, the philosophers give examples: the motion of the sun and the apparent motion of shadows (which is not real motion but an optical effect), and the motion of the two ends of a millstone or compass. The theologians begin their answer by denying that the concomitance is rationally necessary; they accept it only as customary, meaning that God could create the motions without necessary connection. The answer is cut off, implying that the full rebuttal continues beyond this page.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: They do: they deny that the concomitance is rationally necessary, affirming that God can create motions without necessary connections, so rests are possible.

Key Takeaway
The theologians counter the philosophers' objections by denying that motion concomitance is rationally necessary, preserving the possibility of slowness via intervening rests as part of divine custom.

Second Argument on Slowness and Causality
Presents philosophers' objection and theologians' answer on causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that slowness contradicts causality and the theologians' response based on God's absolute power as the free agent.

Core Idea
God's will breaks the necessity of natural cause-effect.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents a key objection from the philosophers regarding slow motion and the principle of causality. The philosophers argue that if a cause is fully present, the effect must occur; thus slowness or delay is impossible. The theologians respond by asserting that the true agent is God's power, which can create motion or rest regardless of apparent causes. This establishes the Ash'ari view that all events are directly created by God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer.
2
Identify the Philosophers' Argument
The philosophers claim that in slow motion, the cause is fully present with conditions, so rest cannot occur. Identify the logical structure: if cause complete, effect must follow; otherwise impossible.
They rely on the principle that the effect cannot lag behind a complete cause.
3
Understand the Theologians' Response
The answer shifts the basis from natural causality to God's direct power. Understand that all movements are 'forced' (qasriyya) in the sense of being created by God, not inherent.
This undermines the necessity assumed by the philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن في الحركة البطيئة علة الحركة موجودة بشرائطها، والموانع مرتفعة وإلا امتنعت الحركة، فلو وقع في أنشاء ذلك سكون لزم تخلف المعلول عن تمام العلة وهو محال . وأجيب بأن المؤثر في الحركات، بل في جميع الممكنات قدرة الفاعل المختار، فله أن يوجد الحركة في زمان والسكون في آخر مع كون المتحرك بحالة، غاية الأمر أن جميع الحركات تكون قسرية بمعنى كونها بإيجاد الغير.
Second: In slow motion, the cause of motion exists with its conditions, and the obstacles are removed, otherwise motion would be impossible. So if rest were to occur at that time, the effect (ma‘lūl) would be absent despite the complete cause, and that is impossible. And it was answered: The effective agent in movements, indeed in all possibilities, is the power of the free agent (qādir al-mukhtār); He can bring about motion at one time and rest at another while the moving thing is in the same state, the utmost being that all motions are forced (qasriyya) in the sense of being brought about by another.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This is the second argument (actually the third in sequence) from the philosophers. They argue that in slow motion, the cause of motion is fully present with its conditions and no obstacles, so if rest occurred, the effect (motion) would be absent despite the complete cause – which is impossible (محال). The answer from the theologians is that the real agent for all movements and all contingent things is the power of the free agent (قدرة الفاعل المختار), i.e., God. He can create motion at one time and rest at another while the object remains the same. So the apparent necessity of cause-effect is overridden by divine volition. All motions are therefore 'forced' (قسرية) in the sense of being created by another, not necessarily from the nature of the object. This answers the objection because the cause of motion is not an inherent principle but God's direct intervention.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
قدرة الفاعل المختار
قسرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle that 'effect follows complete cause' applies to God's actions and binds Him.
Correction: The theologians clarify that this principle governs natural causation, not God's power. God can create motion or rest directly, so the principle does not limit Him.

Checkpoint
How do the theologians answer the philosophers' objection about slowness contradicting causality?
A) By denying the principle of causality
B) By asserting that all motions are created directly by God's power
C) By claiming slow motion has no cause
D) By arguing rests are not real

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' objection that slowness contradicts causality is answered by affirming that God's power is the direct cause of all motions and rests, so no necessity binds God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where all natural conditions point to a certain outcome, yet the opposite happens—do you acknowledge that God's will is the true agent, or does your heart still cling to 'natural necessity'? How would you react if, right now, God withheld a seemingly inevitable blessing?
?
The philosophers saw slowness as absurd because causes must produce their effects instantly. In your spiritual life, do you demand that God's responses follow a predictable 'cause-effect' pattern? Are you willing to wait in the 'rest' of faith, trusting that the same God who creates motion can sustain a pause for His wise purpose?
?
If all motions are truly 'forced' by God, then your next heartbeat, your next breath, is directly from Him. Does this awareness fill you with awe and gratitude, or do you still feel entitled to the natural flow of events? What would change if you lived each moment with the certainty that God is the immediate cause of everything?

The Argument of Perceptual Indistinguishability and Its Answer
Philosophers' objection on perceptual indistinguishability and theologians' response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that intercalated rests would render slow motion imperceptible or mostly stationary, and the theologians' response that motion's existential nature allows it to overpower rests in perception.

Core Idea
Existence (wujūdī) overpowers absence when motion and rest intermingle.

This passage presents a third, more sophisticated objection from the philosophers against the kalam theory that slowness is caused by intercalated rests (sakanāt). Using astronomical proportions, they argue that a horse's run, being extremely slow relative to the celestial sphere, would be imperceptible if composed of more rests than motions. The theologians respond by explaining that motion and rest are not perceived as separate instants but blend together, and because motion is existent (wujūdī), it gradually overwhelms the non-existent rests, becoming perceptible despite their numerical superiority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and its literal English translation. Notice the logical structure: the philosophers present a conditional (if...then) and argue the consequence is false, hence the antecedent must be false.
The passage ends mid-sentence but the core argument and answer are complete.
2
Identify the celestial sphere analogy
The philosophers compare the horse's motion (covering a tiny part of Earth in a day) to the supreme celestial sphere's full daily rotation. Since the horse's motion is minuscule in comparison, it would be extremely slow and thus intercalated with many more rests than motions.
This analogy highlights the huge discrepancy in scale, making the point about imperceptibility.
3
Understand the two possible perceptual outcomes
The philosophers claim that if rests intercalate, either the horse would always appear still (because motions are overwhelmed), or it would appear still most of the time (because rests outnumber motions by a huge ratio). Both contradict our actual perception.
They use a strong reductio ad absurdum against the kalam position.
4
Grasp the theologians' answer
The answer denies that rests and motions are perceptually distinct. Instead, they blend into a single continuous sensation. Motion, being existent, gradually emerges and dominates the rests, which are non-existent, so we see motion despite the many rests.
This reply relies on the kalam distinction between existence (wujūd) and non-existence (ʿadam) and the primacy of existence in perception.
