الثالث : أنه لو كان البطء لتخلل السكنات لزم أن لا يقع الإحساس بشيء من الحركات التي تشاهد في عالم العناصر كعدو الفرس وطيران الطائر ومرور السهم وغير ذلك إلا مشوبة بسكنات هي أضعاف الأفهام واللازم ظاهر الانتفاء، وجه اللزوم أن تلك الحركات لا تقطع في اليوم بليلته إلا بعض وجه الأرض، وجميع الأرض بالنسبة إلى الفلك الأعظم الذي يتم في اليوم بليلته دورة مما ليس له قدر محسوس . وبالجملة : ففي غاية الصغر فتلك الحركات في غاية البطء، فيلزم أن تتخلل حركة الفرس مثلا سكنات بقدر زيادة حركة الفلك الأعظم على حركاته، ويكون الحركات مغمورة لا يحس بها أصلاً، فيرى الفرس ساكناً على الدوام، أو يحس بها في زمان أقل من زمان السكنات بتلك النسبة فيرى ساكناً أضعاف آلاف ما يرى متحركاً ، لأن السكون وان كان عدمياً عندهم، لكن لا خفاء في أن الجسم قد يرى ساكناً ، قد يرى متحركاً ، ويفرق الحس بين الحالين . وأجيب بأن تخلل السكنات بين الحركات وامتزاجها بها ليس بحيث يفرق الحس بين أزمنتها ، بل صارتا بمنزلة شيء واحد إلا أن الحركات لكونها وجودية تظهر على الحس شيئاً فشيئاً فتقهر السكنات وتغلبها، وإن كانت السكنات في
Third: If slowness were due to the intercalation of rests, it would necessitate that no perception occurs of any of the motions witnessed in the elemental world, like the running of a horse, the flight of a bird, the passage of an arrow, etc., except mixed with rests that are many times more numerous – but the necessary consequence is obviously false. The reason for the necessity is that those motions cover in a day and night only a part of the earth's surface, and the entire earth relative to the supreme celestial sphere (al-falak al-a‘ẓam) which completes a revolution in a day and night is something of no perceptible magnitude. In short, they are extremely small, so those motions are extremely slow. Consequently, the horse's motion, for example, would be intercalated with rests in proportion to the increase of the motion of the supreme sphere over its motion, and the motions would be overwhelmed and not sensed at all – so the horse would always be seen as still; or it would be sensed in a time less than the time of the rests in that proportion, so it would be seen as still hundreds of thousands of times more than it is seen as moving. For although rest is non-existent according to them, there is no concealment that a body may be seen as still and may be seen as moving, and the sense distinguishes between the two states. And it was answered: The intercalation of rests among motions and their mixing with them is not such that the sense can distinguish between their times; rather they become like a single thing, except that motions, because they are existent, appear to sense little by little and thus overpower and dominate the rests, even if the rests are ...
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This is the third argument from the philosophers. They argue that if slowness were due to intercalated rests, then any very slow motion in the elemental world (like a horse running, bird flying, arrow shooting) would be imperceptible. The reason is that these motions cover only a tiny distance in a day relative to the celestial sphere's vast daily rotation. The horse's motion would be intermingled with many more rests than actual motion, making it appear always still. Or if sensed, it would be seen as still far more often than moving. The answer from the theologians is that the intercalation of rests is not such that the senses can distinguish separate moments of motion and rest; they blend into a single continuous perception. Motion, being existent (وجودي), gradually appears to the senses and thus overcomes and dominates the rests, even though rests may be more numerous. The passage cuts off, but the reasoning is that motion's existence gives it perceptual priority.

عالم العناصر
فلك أعظم
سكون
وجودي
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Slow motion would be imperceptible if interrupted by many rests.
Correction: The theologians answer that motion and rest blend perceptually; motion's existential quality (wujūdī) allows it to be sensed despite numerical inferiority.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' objection regarding perception of slow motion?
A) Slow motion cannot exist
B) If rests intercalate, slow motion would be imperceptible or perceived as mostly still
C) The senses can detect rests
D) The celestial sphere's motion is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' objection fails because motion's existence (wujūdī) allows it to be perceived even amidst numerous rests through perceptual blending.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas of your spiritual life where you feel you are making no progress—like slow motion—but in reality the existence (the reality of your efforts) is there, gradually overcoming the 'rests' of lethargy? Will you trust that small, consistent acts of worship have real existence that will eventually be perceived?
?
When you look at your own shortcomings, do you focus more on the countless 'rests' (sins, laziness) or on the existent movements of good deeds? How can you shift your perception to reflect the truth that existence overpowers non-existence, as this passage teaches?
?
This lesson shows that mixing of motion and rest can appear as a single continuous thing. Is there a sin or habit you have convinced yourself is 'mixed' with goodness so that you don't address it? What would it mean to let the existent good in you overpower that restful complacency today?

Second Objection: Causal Impossibility of Intermittent Rests
The second objection and its response regarding causality and the free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the causal argument against the theory that slow motion consists of intermittent rests, and how the response invokes the free agent's power.

Core Idea
A full cause cannot be present without its effect; only a free agent can interrupt motion.

The second objection targets the very heart of causality: if all conditions for motion are met, motion must occur. The theory of intermittent rests seems to violate this principle. Let us examine the objection and its theological response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Identify the causal claim: if the cause is complete and obstacles absent, motion cannot be absent. This is a fundamental principle in kalam.
The objection uses 'علة' (cause) in a technical sense.
2
Understand the causal principle
The objection relies on the idea that a complete cause necessitates its effect. In slow motion, the cause of motion is present, so rest is impossible.
3
Analyze the response
The response shifts from natural causality to divine free agency. God, as the free agent, can create motion and rest directly, making all motions 'compulsory' (qasriyya) in the sense of being caused by another.
This response preserves the possibility of intermittent rests without violating causality.
4
Evaluate the debate
Consider the strength of the objection and the response. The author later hints that the responses are weak, but here just present the argument.
This sets up the tension in the next objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن في الحركة البطيئة علة الحركة موجودة بشرائطها، والموانع مرتفعة وإلا امتنعت الحركة، فلو وقع في أنشاء ذلك سكون لزم تخلف المعلول عن تمام العلة وهو محال . وأجيب بأن المؤثر في الحركات، بل في جميع الممكنات قدرة الفاعل المختار، فله أن يوجد الحركة في زمان والسكون في آخر مع كون المتحرك بحالة، غاية الأمر أن جميع الحركات تكون قسرية بمعنى كونها بإيجاد الغير. الثالث : أنه لو كان البطء لتخلل السكنات لزم أن لا يقع الإحساس بشيء من الحركات التي تشاهد في عالم العناصر كعدو الفرس وطيران الطائر ومرور السهم وغير ذلك إلا مشوبة بسكنات هي أضعاف الأفهام واللازم ظاهر الانتفاء، وجه اللزوم أن تلك الحركات لا تقطع في اليوم بليلته إلا بعض وجه الأرض، وجميع الأرض بالنسبة إلى الفلك الأعظم الذي يتم في اليوم بليلته دورة مما ليس له قدر محسوس ففي غاية الصغر فتلك الحركات في غاية البطء، فيلزم أن تتخلل حركة الفرس مثلا سكنات بقدر زيادة حركة الفلك الأعظم على حركاته، ويكون الحركات مغمورة لا يحس بها أصلاً، فيرى الفرس ساكناً على الدوام، أو يحس بها في زمان أقل من زمان السكنات بتلك النسبة فيرى ساكناً أضعاف آلاف ما يرى متحركاً، لأن السكون وإن كان عدمياً عندهم، لكن لا خفاء في أن الجسم قد يرى ساكناً، قد يرى متحركاً، ويفرق الحس بين الحالين . وأجيب بأن تخلل السكنات بين الحركات وامتزاجها بها ليس بحيث يفرق الحس بين أزمنتها، بل صارتا بمنزلة شيء واحد إلا أن الحركات لكونها وجودية تظهر على الحس شيئاً فشيئاً فتقهر السكنات وتغلبها، وإن كانت السكنات في غاية الكثرة، فيرى الفرس متحركاً على الدوام، ولا يخفى على المنصف قوة الأدلة، وضعف الأجوبة .
The second (objection): that in slow motion, the cause of motion exists with its conditions, and obstacles are removed, otherwise motion would be impossible. So if rest occurs during that, it would necessitate the effect's lagging behind the complete cause, which is impossible. And it was answered that the effective factor in motions, nay in all possible things, is the power of the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār). So He can create motion at one time and rest at another while the moving thing remains the same, the only result being that all motions become compulsory (qasriyya) in the sense of being brought about by another. The third (objection): that if slowness were due to the intermingling of rests (sakanāt), it would necessitate that no sensation occurs of any of the motions observed in the elemental world, such as the running of a horse, the flight of a bird, the passing of an arrow, and others, except mixed with rests that are many times beyond comprehension, yet the consequent is obviously false. The reason for the necessity is that those motions do not traverse in a day and night except a small part of the earth's surface, and the whole earth relative to the highest sphere (al-falak al-aʿẓam) which completes a revolution in a day and night is something of no sensible magnitude, so it is extremely small, thus those motions are extremely slow. Consequently, the motion of a horse, for example, would be interspersed with rests in proportion to the excess of the highest sphere's motion over its motions, and the motions would be overwhelmed, not sensed at all, so the horse would be seen as perpetually at rest, or it would be sensed in a time less than the time of the rests by that proportion, so it would be seen at rest thousands upon thousands of times more than it is seen moving. For rest, even though it is non-existent (ʿadami) according to them, there is no doubt that a body may be seen at rest and may be seen moving, and sense distinguishes between the two states. And it was answered that the intermingling of rests among motions and their blending with them is not such that sense distinguishes their times; rather, they become like a single thing, except that motions, because they are existent, appear to sense little by little, so they overcome and dominate the rests, even if the rests are extremely numerous. Thus the horse is seen moving perpetually, and it is not hidden from the fair-minded person the strength of the arguments and the weakness of the answers.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the theory that slow motion (al-baṭʾ) is caused by intermittent rests (takhallul al-sakanāt) interspersed between motions, along with their responses. The first objection (the second overall) argues that if a cause of motion is fully present and obstacles are absent, motion must occur; the occurrence of rest would imply that the effect fails to follow its complete cause, which is impossible. The response invokes the power of the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār): God can create motion at one moment and rest at another without any change in the object, making the motion 'compulsory' in the sense of being directly caused. The second objection (the third) is a perceptual argument: if slow motion consisted of many rests, then extremely slow motions like the horse's run would be interspersed with so many rests that the motion would be imperceptible, yet we clearly see the horse moving. The response explains that the rests are so brief and blended with motion that sensation cannot distinguish them; since motion is existent (wujūdī), it predominates, and the object appears continuously moving. The author concludes that the arguments for the original theory are strong but the answers are weak.

Key Terms
علة
فاعل مختار
قسرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the causal principle 'complete cause necessitates effect' applies to God's actions in the same way as physical causes.
Correction: In kalam, God is the free agent who can create motion or rest without change in the object; the causal principle only holds for natural causes, not for the divine will.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the second objection?
A) The horse would appear stationary.
B) Rest cannot occur because the complete cause of motion is present.
C) The highest sphere's motion is too fast.
D) Sensation cannot distinguish rests.

Key Takeaway
The second objection relies on the principle that a complete cause cannot be present without its effect; the response counters by asserting God's free agency, making causality secondary to divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every motion and rest in your life is directly created by God, why do you often attribute your achievements to your own effort and your failures to external circumstances? How would your daily routine change if you truly internalized this truth?
?
The objection assumed that a complete cause must produce its effect. Where in your life do you assume that your efforts (a complete cause) guarantee results? Can you identify one area where you need to surrender that illusion and trust in God's free agency?
?
If all your actions are 'compulsory' in the sense that God creates them, what room is left for your personal responsibility? How does this knowledge affect the way you approach repentance and reliance on God today?

Third Objection: Perceptual Impossibility of Intermittent Rests
The third objection based on sensation and the response about predominance of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the perceptual argument that if slow motion were full of rests, very slow motions would appear completely stationary, and the response that motion predominates over rest in sensation.

Core Idea
If rests outnumber motions, we would see objects at rest, not moving.

The third objection brings in empirical observation: we clearly see horses run and birds fly. If their motion were interwoven with countless rests, they should appear stationary. How can this be reconciled with the intermittent rests theory? Let's examine the argument and its response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its reasoning
Read the passage carefully. Note the reasoning: slow motions like a horse's run are extremely slow compared to the highest sphere, so if slowness comes from rests, the rests would vastly outnumber the motions, making the motion imperceptible.
The objection uses a comparative scale with the highest sphere (al-falak al-aʿẓam).
2
Understand the analogy with the highest sphere
The earth's surface moves very little in a day relative to the highest sphere's full rotation. So earthly motions are extremely slow. If slowness is due to rests, the rests would be many times more than motions, swamping them.
3
Grasp the response about sensation being overwhelmed
The response argues that the rests are so brief and blended that sensation cannot distinguish them individually. Motion, being existent (wujūdī), gradually impresses upon the senses and overcomes the non-existent rests.
The response emphasizes that existence (wujūd) has a perceptual advantage over non-existence (ʿadam).
4
Reflect on the author's concluding remark
The author concludes that the arguments (i.e., the objections) are strong and the answers weak, indicating his own view leans towards the original theory.
This gives insight into the author's position within the debate.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The 'Now' as an Imaginary Extremity of Time
Initial refutation of the 'now' as a real entity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the 'now' (al-ān) is considered an imaginary extremity of time with no external reality.

Core Idea
The 'now' is like a point on a line—imaginary, not real.

The author begins by engaging with the opponent's view that the 'now' is an extremity of time analogous to a point on a line. This lesson unpacks that analogy and shows why the 'now' cannot be a real container for motion or arrival.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's position
Start by reading the Arabic passage that presents the opponent's view: after conceding no part, they claim the 'now' is like a point, unreal unless time is cut off.
2
Understand the analogy
Notice how the 'now' is compared to a point: just as a point has no extension, the 'now' has no duration. Ask what this implies for motion or arrival occurring 'in' it.
3
Follow the author's objections
The author argues that if the 'now' is purely imaginary, then arrival cannot happen in it. Also, if it is a time divisible only by imagination, two 'nows' could coincide.
This undermines the opponent's claim about successive 'nows'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرد عليه بعد تسليم نفي الجزء وثبوت كون الميل علة موجبة للوصول لا معدة ليلزم بقاؤه معه أن الآن عندكم طرف للزمان بمنزلة النقطة للخط فلا تحقق له في الخارج ما لم ينقطع الزمان، وإنما هو موهوم محض بما يفرض للزمان من الانقسام، فكيف يقع فيه الوصول أو الرجوع، وإن أردتم به زماناً لا ينقسم إلا بمجرد الوهم، فلا ثم تغاير آني الميلين لجواز أن يقعا في آن واحد بحسب ماله من الانقسام الوهمي. ولو سلم فلا تم استحالة تتالي الأنين بهذا المعنى، وإنما يستحيل لو لزم منه وجود الجزء. أعني ما لا ينقسم بالوهم أيضاً، ولا خفاء في ضعف المنع الأول، وفي أنهم يعنون بالآن ما لا ينقسم أصلاً حيث يعللون استحالة تتالي الآنات باستلزامه وجود الجزء، وكأنهم يجعلون انقسام الزمان إلى الماضي والمستقبل كافياً في تحقق الآن أعني الطرف الذي يكون نهاية الماضي وبداية المستقبل، ويحكمون على كثير من الأشياء بأنها آنية لا زمانية. فإن قيل: ما بال تحقق الآن لم يستلزم وجود الجزء وتتالي الآنين استلزمه قلنا: لأنه على تقدير التتالي يكون الامتداد الذي هو مقدار الحركة المنطبقة على المسافة متألفاً من الآنات بزيادة واحد واحد، ولا كذلك تحقق طرف للزمان هو عرض قائم به غير حال فيه حلول السرايان، وهذا كما أن ثبوت النقطة لا يستلزم الجزء، وكون الخط متألفاً من نقطة تستلزمه. وقد يقال لو صحت الحجة المذكورة لزم تتالي الآنات أو تخلل السكنات في كل حركة مستقيمة سيما إذا كانت على أجسام منضودة، أو كان المتحرك لا يماس المسافة إلا بنقطة نقطة على التوالي كما إذا أدرنا كرة على سطح مستو أو ركبناها على دولاب دائر فوقه سطح مستو فإن أن الوصول إلى كل نقطة يغاير أن اللاوصول عنه فيجاب بأن انقسام المسافة ههنا سواء كانت على جسم واحد أو أجسام مختلفة محض توهم فلا تحقق للنقطة والآن بخلاف ما إذا انقطعت الحركة فتحققت لها نهاية، فإنه لابد من ذلك في المسافة أيضاً لانطباقها عليه وفيه نظر لا يخفى.
He responds after conceding the negation of the part and the affirmation that inclination is an efficient cause for arrival, not a preparatory one, so that its persistence with it would necessitate that the 'now' with you is an extremity of time like a point to a line, so it has no realization externally unless time is cut off. It is merely a pure imaginary [thing] by what is supposed for time of division, so how can arrival or return occur in it? And if you mean by it a time that is not divisible except by mere imagination, then there is no difference between the two 'nows' of the two inclinations due to the possibility that they occur in one 'now' according to its imaginary division. And if it is conceded, then the impossibility of succession of 'nows' in this sense does not hold; rather, it would be impossible only if it entailed the existence of the part, i.e., what is not divisible even by imagination. And there is no hiddenness regarding the weakness of the first objection, and that they mean by 'now' what is not divisible at all, since they justify the impossibility of succession of 'nows' by its entailing the existence of the part. And it is as if they make the division of time into past and future sufficient for the realization of the 'now', i.e., the extremity which is the end of the past and the beginning of the future, and they judge many things as instantaneous (āniyya) not temporal (zamāniyya). For it is said: Why is it that the realization of the 'now' does not entail the existence of the part, whereas the succession of two 'nows' entails it? We say: Because on the assumption of succession, the extension which is the measure of motion applied to the distance would be composed of 'nows' by addition one by one, and not so the realization of an extremity of time which is an accident subsisting in it, not indwelling in it in the manner of permeation. And this is like the fact that the affirmation of a point does not entail the part, whereas the line being composed of points entails it. And it may be said: If the mentioned proof were valid, then succession of 'nows' or interpolation of rests would be necessary in every rectilinear motion, especially if it is upon contiguous bodies, or if the moving thing touches the distance only point by point consecutively, as when we rotate a sphere on a plane surface, or mount it on a rotating wheel above a plane surface, then the arrival at each point differs from the non-arrival from it. Then it is answered: That the division of distance here, whether it is on one body or different bodies, is pure imagination, so there is no realization for the point and the 'now', unlike when motion is cut off, then an end is realized for it; indeed, that is necessary for the distance as well due to its being applied to it. And there is an obvious flaw in this.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument concerning the nature of the 'now' (al-ān) and its relationship to the divisibility of time. The author begins by stating the opponent's position: after conceding that there is no indivisible part and that inclination is an efficient cause (not preparatory), the opponent claims that the 'now' is like a point on a line, having no external reality unless time is cut off. The author then criticizes this, arguing that if the 'now' is merely imaginary, then arrival or return cannot occur in it. He further notes that if one considers it as a time divisible only by imagination, then two 'nows' could coincide, and the impossibility of successive 'nows' does not hold unless it entails an indivisible part. The author points out the weakness of the opponent's first objection. Then an objection is raised: why does a single 'now' not entail a part, while two successive 'nows' do? The author answers (qulnā) that succession would compose an extension from 'nows', unlike a single extremity. He illustrates with the point and line analogy. A further objection suggests that the mentioned proof would necessitate succession of 'nows' or rests in any rectilinear motion, especially with contiguous bodies or point-by-point contact. The author answers that the division of the distance in such cases is purely imaginary, so there is no real point or 'now', unlike when motion ceases. Finally, he notes an obvious flaw in this answer.

Key Terms
الآن
النقطة
الخط
الوهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the 'now' is a real, indivisible unit of time like a point is a real object.
Correction: The author argues that the 'now' has no external reality; it is merely an imaginary extremity, like a point on a line.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can arrival not occur in the 'now'?
A) Because the 'now' is an indivisible part of time.
B) Because the 'now' is purely imaginary and has no external reality.
C) Because motion requires a duration.
D) Because time is continuous.

Key Takeaway
The 'now' is not a real container for events; it is an imaginary boundary of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the 'now' is merely an imaginary boundary, what does that mean for your habit of postponing deeds to a 'better time'? Are you waiting for a real moment that never arrives?
?
The author denies that arrival can occur in an imaginary 'now'. Have you treated moments of spiritual insight or repentance as final 'arrivals' rather than ongoing processes? What would it mean to stop seeking a perfect 'now' and instead act continuously?
?
Since the 'now' is not a real container, your past and future are also constructs. Does this thought expose any anxiety or regret you hold? Will you let go of them and focus on what is truly real—your present action?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
7 min
Why Successive 'Now's Entail a Part but a Single 'Now' Does Not
Response to the objection about part entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the succession of two 'nows' would imply an indivisible part, while a single 'now' does not.

Core Idea
A single 'now' is a boundary; successive 'nows' compose an extension.

The opponent raises a key question: if a single 'now' does not require a part, why would two successive 'nows' require one? The author clarifies the difference between a boundary and a composition, using the analogy of points and lines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the question: why does a single 'now' not entail a part, but two successive 'nows' do? This is the core puzzle.
2
Study the author's answer
The author explains that succession would compose an extension from 'nows', whereas a single 'now' is merely a terminal point of time, like a point at the end of a line.
3
Apply the point-line analogy
A point at the end of a line does not make the line composed of points, but if you try to build a line from points, you get a part. Similarly, a single 'now' is a limit; successive 'nows' would be building blocks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why does the author say a succession of two 'nows' entails a part, but a single 'now' does not?
A) Because the 'now' is always imaginary anyway.
B) Because succession would compose an extension from 'nows', like a line from points.
C) Because time is continuous and cannot be cut.
D) Because motion requires two instants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often treat the present moment as a mere bridge between past and future. Yet this lesson teaches that a single 'now' is a boundary, not a part you can live in. Are you actually living in the 'now' as a full constituent of your life, or are you allowing it to slip away as a mere divider between what was and what will be? What will you do today to make this 'now' a substantial part of your existence?
?
The author argues that a succession of 'nows' would make time composed of parts, implying that your life is made of such parts. Do you see your days as disconnected instants or as a meaningful extension? What part of your past 'nows' could you not afford to have been meaningless? Commit to a concrete change in how you invest your next 'now'.

Refuting the Objection from Motion and Point Contact
Response to motion-based objection about 'nows' and points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection from point-by-point motion does not force the reality of 'nows' or parts.

Core Idea
Imaginary division of distance does not create real points or 'nows'.

A further objection argues that practical examples of motion (like a sphere rotating on a surface) seem to require real points and 'nows'. The author responds that these divisions are purely imaginary, and only when motion stops does a real endpoint appear.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from motion
The objector says that in cases like a sphere rotating on a plane, the moving object touches each point consecutively, so there must be distinct 'nows' for each point.
2
Understand the author's answer
The author argues that the division of the distance into points is pure imagination (وهم محض). There is no real point or 'now' in continuous motion.
3
Note the exception
Only when motion is cut off (motion ends) does a real end appear, both for time and distance.
4
Evaluate the 'obvious flaw'
The author ends by noting an obvious flaw in the answer, inviting deeper reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception: That physical point contact in motion (e.g., a sphere touching a plane) proves the existence of real 'nows' and points.
Correction: The author argues such division is purely imaginary; no real point or 'now' exists in continuous motion.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when does a real endpoint for distance appear?
A) When motion is continuous.
B) Only when motion is cut off.
C) When the moving object touches each point.
D) Never, because all division is imaginary.

Key Takeaway
Imaginary division of motion does not create real 'nows'; only cessation of motion yields real endpoints.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever imagine your spiritual journey as a series of discrete checkpoints, each a 'now' to be crossed? How might that illusion of many steps distract you from the reality of continuous turning to Allah? What would it mean for you to see your life as one uninterrupted motion toward Him, not fragmented into points?
?
When you consider your past sins, do you view them as isolated points or as a continuous motion away from Allah? How does the lesson's teaching about imaginary division challenge your tendency to fragment your repentance? Will you today treat your return to Allah as a continuous movement, not a checklist of stops?
?
The objector assumed every point of contact matters. In your daily worship, do you obsess over imagined 'points' of perfection, forgetting that the real end is only when Allah cuts off your motion? What would change if you saw your deeds as one continuous intention, not a series of moments to be perfected?

Al-Jubba'i's Argument on Stone Motion and Its Refutation
The author presents al-Jubba'i's argument about the motion of a stone and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's argument that a stone must rest between ascent and descent due to equilibrium of inclinations, and the author's refutation that such rest is merely the instantaneous cessation of motion.

Core Idea
Equilibrium demands rest, but only in an instant—not as a real state.

Al-Jubba'i, a prominent Mu'tazilite theologian, argued that the motion of a stone involves opposing forces that necessitate a moment of equilibrium and hence rest. The author refutes this by conceding that rest in the sense of absence of motion at an instant is admitted by all, and that the transition is instantaneous, not a duration of rest. This lesson unpacks the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: al-Jubba'i's claim, then the author's response (jawāb), and an elaboration (yaʿnī).
2
Identify the core claims
Identify al-Jubba'i's claim about inclinations and equilibrium, and the two parts of the author's refutation: first concession, then the instantaneous point.
The refutation uses the concept of 'ān (instant).
3
Reflect on the refutation's force
Consider why the author's answer undermines al-Jubba'i's argument: if rest is only in an instant, it is not a real state but merely the boundary between motions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وزعم الجبائي إن صعود الحجر بغلبة اعتماده المجتلب على اللازم وهبوطه بالعكس وبينهما لا محالة تعادل يقتضي السكون لامتناع الترجح بلا مرجح. والجواب: أنه لو سلم التعادل ففي أن الوصول ولزوم السكون بمعنى عدم الحركة لو في آن مما لا نزاع فيه. يعني أنه ثبت السكون بين الحركة الصاعدة والهابطة تمسكاً بأن الحجر مثلاً إنما يصعد بسبب أن اعتماده المجتلب، أعني الميل القسري يغلب اعتماده اللازم أعني الميل الطبيعي ثم لا يزال يضعف بمصادمات الهواء المخروق إلى أن يغلب اللازم فيرجع الحجر هابطا والانتقال من الغالبية إلى المغولبية لا يتصور إلا بعد التعادل، وعنده يجب السكون، إذ لو تحرك فإما قسراً أو طبعاً، وكل منهما ترجح بلا مرجح. والجواب: أنه لو سلم لزوم التعادل فيلكن في أن الوصول لا في زمان يبين آني الوصول والرجوع يكون الجسم فيه ساكناً على ما هو المدعي وإن سمي عدم الحركة في الآن سكوناً كان معنى الكلام أن الحركة الأولى تنقطع وتنعدم فتحدث بعدها حركة أخرى وهذا مما لا يتصور فيه نزاع.
He said: And al-Jubba'i claimed that the ascension of a stone is due to the predominance of its acquired inclination over the necessary [natural] inclination, and its descension is the opposite, and between them there is inevitably an equilibrium that necessitates rest, because of the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator. The answer: If equilibrium is conceded, then the arrival and the necessity of rest in the sense of absence of motion, even if in an instant, is something about which there is no dispute. That is, he affirms that rest occurs between the ascending and descending motion, adhering to the fact that a stone, for example, ascends because its acquired inclination, meaning the compulsory inclination, overcomes its necessary inclination, meaning the natural inclination; then it continues to weaken due to collisions with the air that is cut through until the necessary [inclination] overcomes, so the stone descends, and the transition from predominance to being overcome is inconceivable except after equilibrium, and according to him rest is necessary, because if it moved, it would be either compulsory or natural, and each of them is a preponderance without a preponderator. And the answer: If the necessity of equilibrium is conceded, then the arrival is not in time, rather the instantaneous arrival and return, the body is at rest in that [instant] according to what is claimed, and if the absence of motion in the instant is called rest, then the meaning of the statement is that the first motion cuts off and ceases, then another motion occurs after it, and this is not something about which there is dispute.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage presents al-Jubba'i's argument regarding the motion of a stone and the author's refutation. Al-Jubba'i claims that a stone ascends due to the predominance of its acquired inclination (al-iʿtimād al-mujtalab, i.e., compulsory inclination) over its necessary inclination (al-iʿtimād al-lāzim, i.e., natural inclination), and descends when the opposite occurs. Between these two states, there must be a moment of equilibrium (taʿādul), which necessitates rest (sukūn) because preponderance (tarajjuḥ) without a preponderator (murjīḥ) is impossible. The author first answers (al-jawāb) that even if equilibrium is conceded, the rest in an instant (ān) is admitted by all; it is merely the absence of motion in an instant. Then the author clarifies (yaʿnī) that al-Jubba'i's argument holds that rest occurs between the two motions, explaining the mechanism of the stone's motion and the necessity of equilibrium. Finally, the author gives a second answer: if the necessity of equilibrium is conceded, the arrival is not in time but rather instantaneous, and in that instant the body is at rest. Calling this absence of motion 'rest' simply means the first motion ceases and another begins, which is uncontroversial.

Key Terms
الجبائي
الاعتماد المجتلب
الاعتماد اللازم
الميل القسري
الميل الطبيعي
التعادل
السكون
الآن
الترجح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That rest between motions is a real, durational state requiring a cause.
Correction: The rest is only in an instant (the boundary between motions), which is not a real state but a conceptual point. The cessation of one motion and beginning of another is sequential, not a separate state of rest.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i's argument, why must the stone be at rest between ascent and descent?
A) Because the stone's weight changes.
B) Because equilibrium of inclinations necessitates rest due to impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator.
C) Because air resistance stops the stone.
D) Because it is impossible for motion to change direction instantly.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's argument for rest between opposing motions is refuted because such rest is merely the instantaneous boundary between motions, not a durational state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you assume a stable 'equilibrium'—a state that feels unchanging—when in reality, it is only an instant between changes that Allah alone controls? How does that awareness affect your reliance on Him?
?
Al-Jubba'i argued for a necessary rest from his reasoning, but the author showed it was only an instant. Where have you confidently reasoned yourself into a comfortable certainty that may be based on an incomplete understanding? Will you submit to the possibility that your 'rest' is merely the pause before Allah's next command?
?
Consider a habit or belief you hold as 'settled'. Is it truly settled, or is it only an instant between two motions—before it changes or is taken away? How should this truth move you to action today?

Al-Jubbā'ī's Argument on Motion and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani reports and refutes al-Jubbā'ī's argument on the stone's motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubbā'ī's claim that a stone must rest at the peak of its trajectory due to equilibrium of forces, and how al-Taftazani refutes it by distinguishing temporal rest from an instantaneous transition.

Core Idea
Motion's peak is not a pause but an instant transition.

Al-Jubbā'ī, a prominent Mu'tazilite theologian, argued that a thrown stone must come to a complete rest at the highest point of its flight because the upward forced inclination and downward natural inclination balance each other. This seems intuitive, but al-Taftazani shows that the supposed 'rest' is merely the absence of motion in a single indivisible instant—not a duration. Understanding this distinction is key to debating causality and preponderance in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Jubbā'ī's claim
Carefully read the first part of the passage where al-Jubbā'ī explains why the stone must rest at the peak: the equilibrium of forced and natural inclinations prevents motion without a preponderant cause.
Notice that he uses the principle that any motion requires a preponderant cause (tarjīḥ).
2
Identify the refutation's key move
Al-Taftazani’s response grants the equilibrium but reinterprets the notion of rest. Find where he says that the rest in question is merely the absence of motion in an instant (ān).
This is the crucial distinction: rest as a period vs. rest as an instant.
3
Understand the mechanism
The passage explains that the forced inclination weakens due to air resistance until the natural inclination dominates. The transition between the two states cannot happen except after equilibrium, but that equilibrium is instantaneous.
The word 'ān (instant) is a technical term in kalām meaning a dimensionless point in time.
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on why this distinction matters: al-Jubbā'ī's argument would imply a temporal rest, which would require a cause for the stone to resume motion. By denying temporal rest, al-Taftazani avoids that problem.
This debate touches on deeper issues of occasionalism and causality in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (وزعم الجبائي) إن صعود الحجر بغلبة اعتماده المجتلب على اللازم وهبوطه بالعكس وبينهما لا محالة تعادل يقتضي السكون لامتناع الترجح بلا مرجح . والجواب : أنه لو سلم التعادل ففي أن الوصول ولزوم السكون بمعنى عدم الحركة لو في آن مما لا نزاع فيه . يعني أنه ثبت السكون بين الحركة الصاعدة والهابطة تمسكاً بأن الحجر مثلاً إنما يصعد بسبب أن اعتماده المجتلب، أعني الميل القسرى يغلب اعتماده اللازم أعني الميل الطبيعي ثم لا يزول يضعف بمصادمات الهواء المخروق إلى أن يغلب اللازم فيرجع الحجر هابطا والانتقال من الغالبية إلى المغولبية لا يتصور إلا بعد التعادل، وعنده يجب السكون، إذ لو تحرك فإما قسراً أو طبعاً، وكل منهما ترجح بلا مرجح . والجواب : أنه لو سلم لزوم التعادل فيلكن في أن الوصول لا في زمان يبين آني الوصول والرجوع يكون الجسم فيه ساكناً على ما هو المدعي وأن سمى عدم الحركة في الآن سكوناً كان معنى الكلام أن الحركة الأولى تنقطع وتنعدم فتحدث بعدها حركة أخرى وهذا مما لا يتصور فيه نزاع .
He said: (And al-Jubbā'ī claimed) that the rising of the stone by the predominance of its acquired inclination over the necessary inclination, and its descent by the opposite, and between them inevitably there is an equilibrium that necessitates rest, because of the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderant cause. The answer: if equilibrium is granted, then [the issue is] that the moment and the relation of rest in the sense of absence of motion, even in an instant, is not disputed. That is, rest is [supposedly] established between the ascending and descending motion on the basis that the stone ascends because its acquired inclination, i.e., forced inclination, predominates over its necessary inclination, i.e., natural inclination, then it does not cease but weakens by collisions with the pierced air until the necessary inclination predominates and the stone returns descending, and the transition from predominance to being predominated is inconceivable except after equilibrium, and at that point rest is necessary, for if it moved, it would be either by force or by nature, and each is a preponderance without a preponderant cause. The answer: if the necessity of equilibrium is granted, then [it is] that the arrival [occurs] not in time, but rather between the moment of arrival and the moment of return the body is at rest, as is claimed, and if the absence of motion in the instant is called rest, the meaning is that the first motion cuts off and ceases, and another motion occurs after it, and this is not subject to dispute.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from al-Jubbā'ī and its refutation. Al-Jubbā'ī argues that a stone thrown upward must come to rest at the peak of its trajectory because the forced inclination (from the throw) weakens and the natural inclination (gravity) strengthens, and at the point of equilibrium neither predominates, so motion cannot occur without a preponderant cause. The response refutes this by distinguishing between a temporal rest and a merely instantaneous cessation: the stone does not have a duration of rest; rather, the transition between ascending and descending is instantaneous, and calling that instant 'rest' is merely naming the absence of motion in that instant, which is uncontroversial. The passage explains the mechanism: the forced inclination is weakened by air resistance until the natural inclination takes over, but the exact moment of transition is not a period of time but a dimensionless instant.

الاعتماد اللازم
الميل القسري
الميل الطبيعي
التعادل
السكون
الترجح بلا مرجح
آن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Jubbā'ī is arguing for a literal pause in motion lasting time, during which the stone is stationary for a measurable duration.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that the supposed rest is merely an instantaneous state—the absence of motion in a single indivisible instant (ān)—not a period of time.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubbā'ī, what necessitates a state of rest between upward and downward motion?
A) The stone's weight
B) The equilibrium of forced and natural inclinations
C) The resistance of air
D) The stone's inherent desire to stop

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubbā'ī's argument fails because the supposed rest at the peak is merely an instantaneous state, not a duration of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt stuck in a moment of spiritual uncertainty, where you perceived neither progress nor decline? In that instant, do you rely on your own analysis to break the deadlock, or do you trust that Allah controls the transition just as He balances the forces of the stone? Will you commit to surrendering that moment to Him rather than overthinking it?
?
Al-Jubbā'ī insisted that every change must have a cause. Are there areas of your life where you demand a reason for your emotional or spiritual states before you act? How does al-Taftazani's insight that some states are instantaneous—not requiring a cause over time—free you from the burden of always needing an explanation? Can you accept that sometimes you simply need to move, and the cause will be apparent later?
?
The refutation shows that the stone's rest is but an instant without duration. Reflect on a time when you thought you were 'stuck' in a sin or a habit for a period. Could it be that your perception of being stuck is also an instant—a fleeting moment you mistake for a state? How can you, like the stone, allow the next instant to carry you toward descent (repentance) without waiting for a perfect cause?

Four Objections to Intermediate Rest and Their Refutations
Al-Taftazani enumerates four objections to intermediate rest and provides four refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four objections raised against the necessity of an intermediate rest between opposing motions and how al-Taftazani refutes each.

Core Idea
Four absurdities against intermediate rest are countered by clarifying intrinsic force changes and indivisible time.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents the opponent's case that if an intermediate rest were necessary between two opposite motions, it would lead to four absurdities. He then systematically refutes each objection. This lesson walks through each objection and its answer, clarifying key concepts such as natural vs. forced inclination, equilibrium as a cause, and indivisible time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage of objections and answers
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the structure: four objections followed by four answers, then a rephrasing of the first objection and answer.
Notice the use of 'الأول', 'الثاني', etc.
2
Identify each objection and its refutation
For each of the four numbered objections, find the corresponding answer. The answers begin with 'ورد الأول', 'والثاني', etc.
3
Understand the rephrasing of the first objection
Read the additional paragraph starting with 'أي' which restates the first objection and expands on its refutation, emphasizing that forces change intrinsically.
This reinforces the point about intrinsic force changes.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider how the refutations preserve the necessity of intermediate rest by addressing each absurdity without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (احتج) المانع بأنه لو لزم هذا السكون لزم منه محالات . الأول : بقاؤه لبقاء التعادل على أن القاسرة إنما كانت بضعف ممانعة الهواء المخروق. الثاني : وقوعه لا عن سبب لأنه ليس طبيعياً والتقدير عدم القسر والإرادة . الثالث: وقوعه لا في زمان معين لأن كل زمان يفرض فأقل لمنه كاف الرابع : وقوف الجبل الهابط لملاقاة الخردلة الصاعدة . ورد الأول بأن الطبيعية تتدرج إلى القوة والقاسرة إلى الضعف بحسب الذات. ولهذا يكون هبوط الحجر عند القرب من الأرض أقوى. والثاني بأن تعادل القوتين قاسر . والثالث بأنه يقع في زمان لا يقبل الانقسام العقلي، الرابع بأن الخردلة ترجع بمصادمة هواء الجبل قبل أن يلاقيها مع أن وقوفه مستبعد مستحيل) . أي القائل بعدم لزم سكون بين الحركتين بوجوه : لا الأول : أنه لو لزم انتهاء الصاعدة القسرية إلى و مان سكون، لزم بقاؤه من غير تعقب هبوط، لأنه لا سبب لضعف القاسرة إلا مصادمة المخروق، وهي منتفية عند السكون . وأجيب بالمنع ، بل الطبيعة تتدرج إلى القوة، والقاسرة إلى الضعف بحسب الذات، ولهذا تكون حركة الحجر الهابط عند القرب من الأرض أشد ، وما ذكر ابن سينا من أنه لو لا مصادمات الهواء المخروق للقوة القسرية لوصل الحجر المرمي إلى سطح الفلك في حيز المنع .
He said: (The preventer argued) that if this rest were necessary, then absurdities would follow from it. First: its persistence due to the persistence of equilibrium, since the forced [inclination] was only weakened by the resistance of the pierced air. Second: its occurrence without a cause, because it is not natural and the supposition is the absence of force and volition. Third: its occurrence in no specific time, because any time one imagines, a smaller portion of it would suffice. Fourth: the stopping of the falling mountain to meet the rising mustard seed. The first was answered by [saying that] the natural [inclination] increases in strength and the forced [inclination] decreases in strength according to essence. For this reason, the stone's descent is stronger when near the ground. The second: by [saying that] the equilibrium of the two forces is compelling. The third: by [saying that] it occurs in a time that does not accept intellectual division. The fourth: by [saying that] the mustard seed is repelled by the air pressure of the mountain before it meets it, and its stopping is unlikely and impossible. That is, the one who denies the necessity of rest between the two motions [argued] with points: No. First: if the end of the forced ascent leads to a moment of rest, its persistence would follow without subsequent descent, because there is no cause for the weakening of the forced [inclination] except the collision with the pierced [air], and that is absent during rest. And it was answered by denial; rather, the natural [inclination] increases in strength and the forced [inclination] decreases in strength according to essence. For this reason, the motion of the falling stone is more intense when near the ground. And what Ibn Sīnā mentioned, that if there were no collisions of the pierced air with the forced force, the thrown stone would reach the surface of the celestial sphere, is [subject to] denial.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The opponent (al-māni') raises four logical absurdities that would follow if the intermediate rest were real: (1) the rest would persist eternally because the cause of weakening (air resistance) is absent during rest; (2) the rest would be uncaused since it is neither natural nor forced nor voluntary; (3) the rest would have no definite temporal duration; (4) a falling mountain would have to stop to meet a rising mustard seed. The refutations: (1) The natural inclination inherently increases and the forced inclination inherently decreases regardless of air resistance; (2) the equilibrium itself is a cause; (3) the rest occurs in an indivisible instant; (4) the mustard seed is repelled by air pressure before contact. The following paragraph (beginning with 'ay') rephrases the first objection and its answer, emphasizing that the forced inclination weakens inherently, not merely by air resistance, and reports that Ibn Sīnā's claim that without air resistance the stone would reach the celestial sphere is rejected.

Key Terms
محالات
القاسرة
الطبيعة
التعادل
زمان لا يقبل الانقسام
الخردلة
مصادمات الهواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the first objection implies the rest must be permanent, because the cause of weakening (air resistance) is absent during rest.
Correction: The answer clarifies that forced inclination weakens intrinsically, not just due to air resistance, so the rest can end without external cause.

Checkpoint
How was the fourth objection (the falling mountain stopping to meet a rising mustard seed) answered?
A) The mountain never falls
B) The mustard seed is repelled by air pressure before meeting the mountain
C) Both objects would stop simultaneously
D) The objection is invalid because mustard seeds cannot rise

Key Takeaway
The four refutations show that intermediate rest is logically consistent by appealing to intrinsic force changes, equilibrium as a cause, indivisible time, and repulsion before contact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a belief that seems absurd to your reasoning (like the mountain stopping for a mustard seed), do you immediately reject it, or do you pause to consider that your assumptions about how things work might be incomplete? Where in your life have you dismissed something too quickly because it didn't fit your mental framework?
?
The author shows that hidden causes—like intrinsic force changes or air pressure—can resolve apparent absurdities. What hidden causes might you be ignoring in your own spiritual life that, if acknowledged, would make sense of struggles you currently find irrational? Will you ask Allah to open your eyes to those causes today?
?
If you are honest, do you demand that divine truths fit neatly into your human logic, like the opponent demanding a cause for every rest? Are you willing to accept that some realities—like the truth of Allah's attributes or the unseen—occur in ways your mind cannot divide or measure? What would it cost you to surrender that demand for control?

The Second Objection: Rest from Equilibrium
Second objection and its answer about rest from equilibrium.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the equilibrium of two forces can cause rest and why this rest does not require a separate cause.

Core Idea
Equilibrium of forces is itself a force toward rest until nature prevails.

The second objection argues that if rest were necessary, it would be either natural (false) or forced, but no force exists. The answer introduces a key kalam concept: equilibrium of two forces acts as a forcing power that produces rest temporarily. Ibn Sina and the Imam are cited to clarify that this rest, being necessary, needs no further cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage for the second objection and its answer. Notice how the objection assumes no force for rest, and the answer redefines equilibrium as that force.
Pay attention to the reference to Ibn Sina.
2
Understand the concept of equilibrium as a force
The answer claims that equilibrium (تعادل القوتين) itself is a forcing power (قوة قاسرة) that causes rest until nature overcomes. This is a subtle point in kalam physics.
3
Grasp the implication about causality
The Imam's statement indicates that necessary concomitants do not require a distinct cause. Reflect on how this connects to theological causation.
This is a key principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه لو لزم لكان إما سكوناً طبيعياً وهو ظاهر البطلان، وإما قسرياً، والتقدير عدم القاسر إلى السكون . وأجيب بأن تعادل القوتين قاسر إلى السكون إلى أن تغلب الطبيعة، وفي كلام ابن سينا أن القوة القاسرة مسكنة للجسم في بعض الأحيان ، وإلى أحد هذين المعنيين ينظر ما قال الإمام : أن هذا السكون لما كان ضروري الحصول لم يستدع علة كسائر اللوازم . الثالث : أنه لو لزم لضرورة تعادل القوتين أو استحالة تتالي الآنين، امتنع كونه في زمان ما لأن كل زمان نفرض فأقل منه كاف في دفع تلك الضرورة. وأجيب بأنه يقع في زمان لا يقبل الانقسام إلا بمجرد الوهم، لأنه الذي يمتنع أن يكون بعضه مقداراً للسكون وبعضه لا . الرابع : إنه يستلزم وقوف الجبل الهابط بملاقاته الخردلة الصاعدة. أجيب بأن الخردلة ترجع بمصادمة ريح الجبل، فسكونه يكون قبل ملاقاة الجبل . إن قيل : قد نشاهد أن الملاقاة كانت حال الصعود دون الرجوع، كما في السهم الصاعد بل كما في حركة اليد إلى فوق، فإنه يعلم قطعاً أن الرجوع لم يكن إلا بعد الملاقاة . قلنا : لو سلم فوقوف الجبل مستبعد لا مستحيل .
Second: that if it were necessary, it would be either natural rest—which is clearly false—or forced rest, while the assumption is that there is no force toward rest. It was answered that the equilibrium of the two forces is a force toward rest until nature overcomes. And in the words of Ibn Sina, the forcing power causes the body to rest in some instances. And to one of these two meanings refers what the Imam said: that this rest, when it is necessarily occurring, does not require a cause like other necessary concomitants. Third: that if it were necessary due to the necessity of the equilibrium of the two forces or the impossibility of successive two moments, it would be impossible for it to occur in any time, because any time we assume, a smaller amount than it suffices to repel that necessity. It was answered that it occurs in a time that does not admit division except by mere imagination, because it is that of which it is impossible that some part be a measure for rest and some part not. Fourth: that it entails the stopping of the descending mountain by its meeting the ascending mustard seed. It was answered that the mustard seed returns due to the collision with the wind of the mountain, so its rest occurs before the meeting with the mountain. If it is said: we observe that the meeting occurred in the state of ascent without return, as in the ascending arrow, rather as in the movement of the hand upward, for it is known certainly that the return did not occur except after the meeting. We say: even if conceded, the stopping of the mountain is unlikely, not impossible.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage contains three consecutive objections and their answers regarding the motion of bodies, followed by an additional objection and response. The second objection argues that if a certain result were necessary, it would be either natural rest (false) or forced rest, but no force to rest exists. The answer states that equilibrium of two forces itself acts as a force to rest until nature prevails, citing Ibn Sina. The third objection claims that if the result were necessary due to equilibrium or impossibility of successive moments, it would be impossible in any time since a smaller time would already repel the necessity. The answer invokes an indivisible instant that only the imagination can divide. The fourth objection says that the result would entail a descending mountain stopping when meeting an ascending mustard seed. The answer explains that the mustard seed returns due to the mountain's wind, so the mountain's rest occurs before contact. Then a further doubt: observation shows that meeting occurs during ascent, not return, as with an arrow or hand. The reply concedes the case but deems it merely improbable, not impossible.

سكون
قوة قاسرة
تعادل القوتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equilibrium of forces always results in motion.
Correction: In kalam physics, equilibrium can result in rest as a forced state until one force prevails.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what causes rest in the case of equilibrium?
A) Natural tendency
B) An external forcing power
C) The equilibrium of the two forces itself
D) The absence of any force

Key Takeaway
Equilibrium of forces produces rest without needing an additional cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a state of 'equilibrium' in your life—a tense standstill between two opposing desires or circumstances—do you recognize it as a forced rest from Allah, or do you think nothing is happening? How can you actively submit to this divine pause rather than resisting it?
?
The passage teaches that necessary concomitants require no separate cause. Is your faith in Allah's oneness a necessary truth for you, or do you constantly seek external proofs? If it is necessary, how does that change the urgency with which you affirm it in your heart?
?
Ibn Sina says a forcing power can cause rest. In what situations have you felt spiritually compelled to be still—perhaps through trials or lack of options—and how did that forced rest eventually give way to a deeper reliance on Allah?

The Third Objection: Rest in an Indivisible Instant
Third objection about temporal impossibility and answer using indivisible instant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that rest due to equilibrium must occur in an indivisible instant, and why this is possible.

Core Idea
Rest can occur in an indivisible instant that only the imagination can divide.

The third objection claims that if rest were necessary due to equilibrium or the impossibility of successive moments, it would be impossible in any divisible time. The answer introduces a key kalam concept: the indivisible instant (الآن) that does not admit division except in the imagination. This allows rest to occur without requiring a duration.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic for the third objection and its answer. Note how the objection uses the idea of any time being too large, and the answer posits an indivisible instant.
2
Understand the indivisible instant
The answer says rest occurs in a time that cannot be divided except by mere imagination (وهم). This is the concept of 'the now' (الآن) in kalam atomism.
Compare with the concept of 'moment' in physics.
3
Reflect on the necessity argument
Why does the objection think any time suffices? Because if rest requires equilibrium, a smaller time would also have equilibrium. The answer sidesteps by using an indivisible instant.

The Fourth Objection: The Mountain and Mustard Seed
Fourth objection and follow-up doubt about rest from collision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that rest would lead to absurdities like a mountain stopping for a mustard seed, and how the answer resolves it.

Core Idea
The stopping of a mountain by a mustard seed is unlikely but not impossible in kalam physics.

The fourth objection uses a vivid example: a descending mountain meeting an ascending mustard seed. If rest were necessary at meeting, the mountain would stop. The answer invokes the mustard seed's return due to the mountain's wind, making the mountain's rest occur before contact. A follow-up doubt from experience (arrow, hand) is answered by conceding it is improbable but not impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and main answer
Read the fourth objection and its initial answer. Visualize the scenario: a huge mountain falling, a tiny mustard seed rising, and the claim that the mountain would stop.
2
Understand the answer's mechanism
The answer says the mustard seed turns back because of the mountain's wind, so the mountain's rest occurs before actual contact. This preserves causality.
3
Analyze the additional doubt and reply
The doubt cites common observation (arrow, hand) where meeting occurs during ascent, not after return. The reply says even if conceded, the mountain stopping is improbable, not impossible—a typical kalam distinction.

?

What does your mental reflex reveal about how much you truly trust that 'Allah is over all things competent'? Will you commit to catching yourself the next time you label a divine promise as 'impossible'?
?
The text distinguishes between 'unlikely' and 'impossible'. Think of a situation in your life—a personal struggle, a broken relationship, a financial crisis—that you have been calling 'impossible' to fix. Based on this lesson, is it truly impossible, or merely unlikely? How does shifting that one word change what you should ask of Allah tonight? What specific dua will you make that you have been too afraid to make?
?
The objector relied on repeated observation (the arrow, the hand) to argue that meeting always happens before return. You too rely on your daily experience to judge what can happen. But the kalam reply says even if the observation holds, the improbable remains possible. In your spiritual life, where have you let 'I have never seen it happen' become 'it cannot happen'? What miracle have you stopped hoping for because you trusted your experience more than Allah's power? Will you ask for it again today?

Multiple Movements of a Body: Vector Addition in Kalam
Classification of a body's resultant motion from two or more movements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical kalam thinkers analyzed the resultant motion of a body subject to multiple simultaneous movements, using the example of a person on a ship and analogies to planetary motion.

Core Idea
Multiple motions on a body combine like vectors: they add, subtract, or produce a net direction.

In this passage, the author presents a detailed classification of how a body moves when it is subject to two or more simultaneous movements. He uses the familiar example of a person on a moving ship to illustrate additive, subtractive, and vector addition effects. Remarkably, he also connects this to planetary motion—speed, slowness, station, and retrogression—showing that the same principles apply to celestial bodies. This lesson walks through his classification step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three basic cases
Read the passage's opening statement where it lists the three scenarios for two movements: same direction, opposite directions, and non-opposite directions. Notice how the resultant displacement is described in each case.
The author first states the rule in abstract terms before giving examples.
2
Examine the ship examples
The passage then illustrates each case with a person on a ship. Compare the three scenarios: person moving with the ship, against the ship, and at a right angle (north/ship east). See how the motion appears from an outside observer.
Pay special attention to the opposite-direction case where the person's motion can cancel, slow, or appear retrograde relative to the ship.
3
Connect to planetary motion and multiple directions
The author explicitly links the example to planetary speed, slowness, station, and retrogression. Then he adds a more complex case with four different directions, leading to a net intermediate direction.
This shows the kalam interest in explaining observable celestial phenomena using terrestrial analogies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السابع : [قد يكون للجسم حركتان إلى جهة فيبعد عن المبدأ بقدرهما أو إلى جهتين متقابلتين فيبعد بقدر يفضل إحداهما على الأخرى إن كان وإلا فيسكن، أو غير متقابلتين فيبعد فيهما بقدر الحركتين، وقد يكون له حركات إلى جهات فيتوسطها على نسبة الحركات . ساكناً واحدة كالمتحرك في السفينة إلى الصوب الذي تتحرك إليه السفينة، فيبعد عن المبدأ بقدر الحركتين وإلى جهتين متقابلتين، كالمتحرك في السفينة إلى خلاف جهتها. فإن لم يكن لإحدى الحركتين فضل على الأخرى ترى الشخص في المبدأ، وإن كان فإما لحركة السفينة فيرى بطيئاً، أو لحركة الشخص فيرى راجعاً، وعلى هذا يبنى سرعة الكوكب وبطؤه ووقوفه ورجوعه إلى جهتين غير متقابلتين كالمتحرك شمالاً في سفينة تجري شرقاً، فيبعد إلى الجهتين بقدر الحركتين، وقد يتحرك الجسم إلى جهات مختلفة كحركة الشخص شرقاً في سفينة تدفع شمالاً في ماء يجري غرباً، وبحركة الريح جنوب، فيكون متوسط ما بين تلك الجهات على حسب ما تقتضيه تلك الحركات .
The seventh section: [A body may have two movements toward one direction, so it moves away from the origin by the sum of them; or toward two opposite directions, so it moves away by the amount that one of them exceeds the other, if there is an excess, otherwise it becomes still; or toward two non-opposite directions, so it moves away in them by the amount of the two movements. And it may have movements toward [various] directions, so it becomes intermediate among them according to the proportion of the movements. Still? one: like a person moving in a ship toward the direction that the ship moves toward, so he moves away from the origin by the sum of the two movements; and toward two opposite directions, like a person moving in the ship opposite to its direction. If there is no excess of one movement over the other, you see the person at the origin; and if there is excess, then either due to the movement of the ship he is seen slow, or due to the movement of the person he is seen retrograde. And upon this is based the speed of the planet, its slowness, its station, and its retrogression. And toward two non-opposite directions: like a person moving north in a ship moving east, so he moves away in the two directions by the amount of the two movements. And a body may move toward different directions: like the movement of a person east in a ship that is driven north in water flowing west, and with the movement of the wind south, so it becomes intermediate between those directions according to what those movements require.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the possible movements of a body when influenced by multiple motions. It begins by stating the general rule: a body can have two movements in the same direction, resulting in additive displacement; or in opposite directions, resulting in the net displacement equal to the difference (if any) or rest if equal; or in non-opposite directions, resulting in vector addition. Then it proceeds to give examples. The first example is a person on a ship moving in the same direction as the ship—the person moves away from the origin by the sum of both motions. The second example is a person moving in the opposite direction of the ship—if speeds are equal, the person appears stationary; if ship speed dominates, the person appears slow; if person speed dominates, the person appears retrograde. This is analogized to planetary motion (speed, slowness, station, retrogression). The third example is non-opposite directions: a person moving north on a ship moving east—displacement is at an angle. Finally, the passage describes a case with multiple directions (east, north, west, south) resulting in a net direction determined by the resultant vector.

Key Terms
حركة
جهة
مقابل
سفينة
كوكب
رجوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body can only have one motion at a time.
Correction: This passage shows that multiple simultaneous motions combine vectorially, producing resultant motion that may differ from any individual motion.

Checkpoint
When a person moves in a ship toward the opposite direction of the ship, and the person's speed equals the ship's speed, what is observed?
A) The person moves forward
B) The person stands still relative to the origin
C) The person moves backward
D) The person moves in a diagonal direction

Key Takeaway
The resultant motion of a body under multiple influences follows clear rules of addition and subtraction, applicable to both terrestrial and celestial motions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your daily life: many forces act on you—family, work, faith. Are you like the person on the ship moving in the same direction, opposite, or at an angle? Are you being driven by the 'ship' (external influences) or are you actively moving yourself? How can you ensure that your net direction aligns with your ultimate purpose in this life?
?
The passage analogizes planetary motion to a person on a ship. When you reflect on your spiritual state, do you sometimes appear stationary or retrograde relative to your goal? What 'ships' are carrying you—your habits, environment, or desires? Are you even aware of their direction and speed? Take one specific area where you feel stagnant and ask: what relative motion is causing that appearance?
?
The person on the ship appears slow or retrograde depending on which movement is observed. How often do you judge others' spiritual progress without considering the different 'ships' they are on? Think of someone you have judged as slow or regressing. Can you see the composite motions in their life that you might be ignoring? Make a commitment to view others with the same nuanced perspective this passage teaches you to see planetary motion.

Composite Motion: How Bodies Move in Two Directions
Sanusi classifies the outcomes of two simultaneous motions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a body can have two simultaneous motions and how to determine the resultant effect (distance, station, or intermediate position) based on the directions and relative magnitudes of those motions.

Core Idea
A body's motion can be composite; the resultant depends on direction and magnitude.

This investigation, known as the 'Seventh Investigation', explores the fascinating possibility that a body may be subject to two motions at the same time. Using the simple example of a person on a ship, the author shows how we can predict the overall motion by considering direction and relative strength. This lays the groundwork for understanding celestial phenomena like planetary speed and retrogression.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow from the general claim to specific cases and examples.
2
Identify the three cases of two motions
Look for the three cases: same direction, opposite directions, and non-opposite directions. For each case, note the resultant distance from the starting point.
Pay attention to what happens when opposite motions are equal.
3
Connect to celestial phenomena
The passage states that the explanation of planetary speed, slowness, station, and retrogression is based on this analysis. Reflect on how composite motions explain apparent planetary motion.
This shows the practical application of the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السابع: قد يكون للجسم حركتان إلى جهة فيبعد عن المبدأ بقدرهما أو إلى جهتين متقابلتين فيبعد بقدر يفضل إحداهما على الأخرى إن كان وإلا، فيسكن، أو غير متقابلتين فيبعد فيهما بقدر الحركتين، وقد يكون له حركات إلى جهات فيتوسطها على نسبة الحركات. ساكناً واحدة كالمتحرك في السفينة إلى الصوب الذي تتحرك إليه السفينة، فيبعد عن المبدأ بقدر الحركتين وإلى جهتين متقابلتين، كالمتحرك في السفينة (١) إلى خلاف جهتها. فإن لم يكن لإحدى الحركتين فضل على الأخرى ترى الشخص في المبدأ، وإن كان فإما لحركة السفينة فيرى بطيئاً، أو لحركة الشخص فیری راجعاً، وعلى هذا تبين (٢) مبنى سرعة الكوكب وبطؤه ووقوفه ورجوعه إلى جهتين غير متقابلتين كالمتحرك شمالاً في سفينة تجري شرقاً، فيبعد إلى الجهتين بقدر الحركتين، وقد يتحرك الجسم إلى جهات مختلفة كحركة الشخصي شرقاً في سفينة تدفع شمالاً في ماء يجري غرباً، وبحركة الريح جنوب، فيكون متوسط ما بين تلك الجهات على حسب ما تقتضيه تلك (٣) الحركات .
The seventh investigation: A body may have two motions towards one direction, so it distances from the starting point by the sum of both; or towards two opposite directions, so it distances by the amount that one excels the other if there is an excess, otherwise it becomes stationary; or towards two non-opposite directions, so it distances in both directions by the sum of the two motions. And it may have motions towards multiple directions, so it becomes positioned in the middle according to the proportion of the motions. One station [example]: like a person moving on a ship towards the direction the ship moves, so he distances from the starting point by the sum of the two motions; and towards two opposite directions, like a person on a ship moving opposite to its direction. If neither of the two motions has an excess over the other, you see the person at the starting point; but if there is an excess, then either due to the ship's motion you see him slow, or due to the person's motion you see him returning. Upon this is based the explanation of the speed, slowness, station, and retrogression of a planet. And towards two non-opposite directions, like a person moving north on a ship sailing east, so he distances in both directions by the sum of the two motions. And a body may move in various directions, like a person moving east on a ship propelled north in water flowing west, and with the wind's motion south, so he becomes intermediate among those directions according to what those motions require.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of cases where a body has two simultaneous motions. The author first states the general claim: a body can have two motions towards one direction, resulting in distance equal to the sum; towards opposite directions, resulting in net distance if one motion exceeds the other, otherwise it stays still; or towards non-opposite directions, resulting in distance in both directions by the sum. Then he gives specific examples using a person on a ship. For two motions to the same direction, the body distances by the sum of both motions. For opposite directions, if the motions are equal, the body appears stationary at the starting point; if the ship's motion is greater, the person appears slow; if the person's motion is greater, he appears to return. This illustrates the basis for explaining celestial phenomena like apparent speed, slowness, station, and retrogression. For non-opposite directions (e.g., north and east), the body moves in both directions by the sum of the motions. Finally, he considers multiple directions (e.g., east, north, west, south) and says the body ends up in a position that is the resultant of all motions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body can only have one motion at a time, and motions cannot combine.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that a body can have two simultaneous motions, and their combined effect depends on direction and magnitude.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh investigation, what happens when a person on a ship moves north while the ship sails east?
A) The person moves only north.
B) The person moves only east.
C) The person moves both north and east, distancing by the sum of the motions.
D) The person stays stationary relative to the starting point.

Key Takeaway
A body can have two simultaneous motions, and the resultant effect is determined by whether the directions are same, opposite, or otherwise, using vector-like addition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, what are the two simultaneous 'motions' you are experiencing right now? Are they pulling you in the same direction, opposite directions, or perpendicular? What is the net effect, and are you satisfied with where you are heading?
?
The passage teaches that if two opposite motions are equal, you appear stationary. Have you ever felt spiritually stuck because the pull of the world and the pull of the Hereafter are equally strong in your heart? What change would you need to make so that one motion dominates and you finally move toward your goal?
