The Three Kinds of Rest: Natural, Forced, and Voluntary
Classification of rest into three types with a note on simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of rest based on their cause and why rest does not admit composition.

Core Idea
Rest is simple and cannot be composed, unlike motion.

Before we can discuss the causes of rest, we must first understand its basic categories. The author presents a clear classification of rest into natural, forced, and voluntary, and emphasizes that rest, unlike motion, is inherently simple.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage where the author lists the three types of rest with examples. Notice the clear structure: natural, forced, voluntary.
The examples help distinguish each type.
2
Understand natural rest
The author explains that natural rest does not require an external cause; it arises from the body's own nature. A stone rests on the ground because its nature seeks that place.
This contrasts with motion, which often needs a cause.
3
Note the denial of composition
The author states that composition (tarkīb) is not conceivable in rest. Rest is simple; only motion can be composed of multiple causes or elements.
This point will be crucial for the upcoming objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإطلاق، ولا يتصور في السكون المركب وسكون الإنسان(۱) الإقسام في المكان الطبيعي، وأثر الإرادة ترك إزالته) . لتضاد ما فيه إذ لا عبرة فيه بتضاد الساكن والمسكن والزمان على ما مر ، ولا تعلق للسكون بما منه وما إليه ،قوله ويكون أي السكون طبيعياً كسكون الحجر على الأرض، وقسرياً كسكونه معلقاً في الهواء ، أو إرادياً كوقوف الطير في الهواء، والطبيعي لا يفتقر إلى مقارنة أمر غير طبيعي كما في الحركة بل يستند إلى الطبيعة مطلقاً، لأن الجسم إذا خلى وطبعه لم يكن له بد من موضع معين لا تطلب مفارقته ولا يتصور في السكون تركب، وإنما تعرض البساطة والتركب للحركة مر في البحث اليابق . كما فإن قيل : سكون الإنسان على الأرض مركب من الطبيعي والإرادي قلنا : لا بل هو واحد وإنما يتوهم التعدد في علته، والتحقيق أنها الطبيعية فقط، وأثر الإرادة ترك إزالته إلى الحركة. فإن كلا من الطبيعة والإرادة والقاسر، إنما يصير تمام علة السكون عند عدم رجحان علة الحركة، وهذا بخلاف الحركة، فإنها لما كانت تقبل الشدة والضعف جاز اجتماع علتين على حركة واحدة كما في الحجر المرمى إلى تحت فظاهر أنها ليست من التركيب شيء وإنما هو اشتداد . (۱) في (ج) الأقسام بدلاً من (الإنسان).
and it is not conceivable in composite rest, and the rest of man [is] classification in natural place, and the effect of will is leaving its removal. because of the opposition of what is in it, since there is no consideration in it of the opposition of the resting thing and the place and time as has passed, and rest has no relation to 'from which' and 'to which'. His statement: 'and it is, i.e., rest, either natural like the rest of a stone on the ground, or forced like its rest suspended in the air, or voluntary like the bird's standing in the air. And the natural does not need the accompaniment of something unnatural as in motion, but is based on nature absolutely, because if the body is left to its nature, it cannot but have a specific place whose separation is not sought, and composition is not conceivable in rest; indeed simplicity and composition occur for motion as has passed in the previous investigation. And if it is said: man's rest on the ground is composite of natural and voluntary, we say: No, rather it is one, and multiplicity is only imagined in its cause, and the reality is that it is only natural, and the effect of the will is leaving its removal into motion. For each of nature, will, and compulsion only becomes the complete cause of rest when there is no preponderance of the cause of motion, and this is unlike motion, for since it accepts intensity and weakness, it is permissible for two causes to combine on one motion, as in a stone thrown downward, and it is apparent that this is not from composition at all, but rather it is intensification. (1) In (c): 'categories' instead of 'man'.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage classifies rest (sukun) into three types based on cause and then addresses an objection about composite rest. The author first states that rest does not admit composition and that man's rest is classified in natural place. The effect of will is only to refrain from removing rest. Then he explains that rest is either natural (like a stone on the ground), forced (like a stone suspended in the air), or voluntary (like a bird hovering). Natural rest relies solely on nature without need for an external cause, unlike motion. When a body is left to its nature, it naturally seeks a specific place and does not seek to leave it. Composition (tarkīb) is not conceivable in rest; it is only found in motion. An objection is raised: man's rest on the ground seems to combine natural and voluntary causes. The author replies that the rest itself is single; the multiplicity is only in its cause. In reality, the cause is only nature, and the will's effect is merely to refrain from initiating motion. The complete cause of rest—whether natural, voluntary, or forced—operates only when there is no preponderant cause of motion. This differs from motion, which can have multiple causes combining because motion accepts intensity and weakness, as in a stone thrown downward, where the initial thrust and gravity combine as intensification, not composition. The footnote notes a variant reading: 'categories' instead of 'man'.

طبيعي
قسري
إرادي
تركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that rest can be composed of multiple causes, like motion.
Correction: The author clearly states that rest does not admit composition (tarkīb); it is always simple.

Checkpoint
Which type of rest does NOT require an external cause?
A) Forced rest
B) Voluntary rest
C) Natural rest
D) All types require a cause

Key Takeaway
Rest is classified into three types based on cause, and unlike motion, it is inherently simple without composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The stone rests naturally in its place without striving. In what area of your life are you striving to find rest instead of trusting the natural place Allah has set for you? What would it look like to let go of control and rest in His decree today?
?
The author says rest is simple, not composed. Think about how you often complicate rest—by earning it, planning it, or filling it with distractions. Will you intentionally set aside time today to experience rest as a pure gift from Allah, without adding your own efforts? Make a concrete plan.

Why Rest Cannot Be Composite: Objection and Answer
Objection and answer regarding composite rest with comparison to motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that man's rest seems composite and the author's resolution that the cause is singular.

Core Idea
Apparent multiplicity in cause does not make rest composite.

Having established that rest is simple, the author now addresses a potential objection: doesn't man's rest on the ground involve both natural inclination and will? The author explains why this does not constitute composition in the rest itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection that man's rest on the ground seems to be composite of natural and voluntary causes. Understand the challenge.
This objection directly tests the previous claim that rest is simple.
2
Follow the answer
The author replies that the rest itself is one; multiplicity is only in the cause. The real cause is nature; the will only refrains from removing it.
The key is distinguishing the cause from the effect.
3
Compare with motion
The author contrasts rest with motion: motion can have multiple causes combining because it admits intensity and weakness, as in a stone thrown downward. Rest does not.
This comparison reinforces the uniqueness of rest.

Source Text

Action and Passion as Continuous Change: The Core Definition
The author defines action and passion as continuous change and provides examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the categories of action (an yaf‘al) and passion (an yanfa‘il) in kalam refer to continuous change, not instantaneous events, and how al-Farabi defined them as change and moving.

Core Idea
Action and passion are continuous processes, not static results.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection about infinite regress by clarifying that not all actions and passions are of the continuous type. He then defines the two categories following al-Farabi, emphasizing that they denote ongoing change. You will read the definition and examples that illustrate the nature of these categories.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the objection
The passage begins with an answer to an objection about infinite regress. Read the first few lines to see why the author distinguishes between types of action.
2
Learn al-Farabi's definitions
Al-Farabi defines an yaf‘al as 'taghyīr wa-taḥrīk' (change and moving) and an yanfa‘il as 'taghayyur wa-taḥarruk' (being changed and being moved). Note the active vs. passive nuance.
3
Examine the examples
The author uses the building of a house and the heating of an object to illustrate the continuous, part-by-part nature of these processes. Identify how each example shows the gradual change.
4
Relate to motion
Notice that the species of an yanfa‘il are the species of motion: generation, corruption, growth, decay, alteration, and translocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التسلسل المحال وترتب أمور لا نهاية لها مع كونها غير محصورة بين حاصرين : والجواب : إن ذلك إنما يلزم لو كان كل تأثير وإيجاد حتى الإبداعي الذي لا يفتقر إلى زمان من قبيل أن يفعل وكل تأثر وحصول حتى الدفعي من قبيل أن ينفعل وليس كذلك بل إذا كان الفاعل يغير المنفعل من حال إلى حال على الاتصال والاستمرار فحال الفاعل هو أن يفعل وحال المنفعل أن ينفعل حتى فسر الفارابي أن يفعل بالتغيير والتحريك ، وأن ينفعل بالتغير والتحرك . وقال لا فرق بين قولنا ينفعل وبين قولنا يتغير ويتحرك وأنواع هذا الجنس هي أنواع الحركة، ففي الجوهر الكون والفساد، وفي الكم النمو والاضمحلال وفي الكيف الاستحالة، وفي الأين النقلة وحقيقة أن ينفعل هو تصير الجوهر من شيء إلى شيء، وتغيره من أمر إلى أمر ما دام سالكاً بين الأمرين على الاتصال، فالتكون كابتناء البيت قليلا قليلا وشيئاً شيئاً، وجزءاً جزءاً على اتصال إلى أن يحصل البيت. وعلى هذا قياس البواقي وأن يفعل هو أن ينتقل الفاعل باتصال الفعل على النسب التي له أجزاء ما يحدثه فى المنفعل حين ما ينفعل، فالمسخن حين ما يسخن له نسبته إلى جزء جزء من الحرارة التي تحدث فيما يتسخن ينتقل من نسبته إلى جزء من الحرارة إلى نسبته إلى جزء آخر على الاتصال، وأنواعه على عدد أنواع أن ينفعل فان كل تغير وحركة يقابله تغير وتحريك كالتكوين للتكون، والإفساد للفساد وكذا أنواع الأنواع كالبناء للابتناء والهدم للانهدام وعلى هذا قياس التضاد، فكما أن ينهدم مضاد لأن يبتني، وأن يتسخن لأن يتبرد، وكذلك أن يهدم مضاد لأن يبتني ، وأن يسخن لأن يبرد، وعلى هذا قياس البواقي وقال ابن سينا، إنما أؤثر لفظ أن ينفعل وأن يفعل على الأنفعال والفعل لأنهما قد يقالان للحاصل بعد انقطاع الحركة وأنما المقولة ما كان توجها إلى غاية من وضع أو كيف أو غير ذلك مستقر من حيث هو كلك . ولفظ أن ينفعل وأن يفعل مخصوص بذلك.
The impossible infinite regress and the ordering of things without end while they are not enclosed between two encompassing [limits]: The answer: That only follows if every effect and bringing into existence, even the creative which does not require time, were of the type of 'to act' (an yaf‘al), and every being affected and occurrence, even the instantaneous, were of the type of 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il), but it is not so. Rather, when the agent changes the patient from one state to another continuously and perpetually, the state of the agent is that it acts (an yaf‘al) and the state of the patient is that it is acted upon (an yanfa‘il). Thus al-Farabi interpreted 'to act' (an yaf‘al) as change and moving (al-taghyīr wa-l-taḥrīk), and 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il) as being changed and being moved (al-taghayyur wa-l-taḥarruk). And he said: There is no difference between our saying 'yanfa‘il' and our saying 'it changes and moves' (yataghayyar wa-yataḥarrak). And the species of this genus are the species of motion: in substance, generation and corruption; in quantity, growth and decay; in quality, alteration; in place, translocation. The reality of 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il) is the becoming of the substance from something to something, and its change from one state to another as long as it is passing between the two states continuously. Thus, becoming (al-takawwun) is like the building of a house little by little, thing by thing, part by part, continuously until the house is obtained. And the rest are analogous. And 'to act' (an yaf‘al) is that the agent transfers by the continuity of action according to the relations that it has to the parts of what it produces in the patient at the time of being acted upon. Thus the heater, at the time it heats, has a relation to each part of the heat that occurs in what is being heated; it transfers from its relation to one part of heat to its relation to another part continuously. And its [action's] types are according to the number of types of 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il), for every change and motion is confronted by a change and moving, like making (takwīn) for becoming (takawwun), and corrupting (ifsād) for corruption (fasād), and likewise the species of species like building (binā') for being built (ib tinā'), and demolishing (hadm) for being demolished (inhidām). And the rest are analogous. And similarly for opposition: just as being demolished (yanhadim) is opposite to being built (yabtanī), and being heated (yatasakhkhan) to being cooled (yatabarrad), likewise demolishing (yahdim) is opposite to building (yabnī), and heating (yusakhkhin) to cooling (yubarrid). And the rest are analogous. And Ibn Sina said: I only prefer the expression 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il) and 'to act' (an yaf‘al) over [the nouns] 'being acted upon' (al-inf‘āl) and 'act' (al-fi‘l) because these [nouns] may be said for what has obtained after the cessation of motion, whereas the category is what is a direction towards an endpoint of position or quality or other than that, settled insofar as it is such and such. And the expression 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il) and 'to act' (an yaf‘al) is specifically for that.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is part of an answer to an objection about an impossible infinite regress. The author argues that the regress would only follow if every action and being acted upon were of the type that requires time and change, but that is not the case. He then defines the two categories 'an yaf‘al' (to act) and 'an yanfa‘il' (to be acted upon) as denoting continuous change, following al-Farabi. Al-Farabi interprets 'an yaf‘al' as 'taghyīr' (change) and 'taḥrīk' (moving), and 'an yanfa‘il' as 'taghayyur' (being changed) and 'taḥarruk' (being moved). He states that 'yanfa‘il' is synonymous with 'changing and moving'. The species of this genus are the types of motion: generation and corruption in substance, growth and decay in quantity, alteration in quality, and translocation in place. The reality of 'an yanfa‘il' is illustrated as the gradual change of a substance from one state to another, like building a house part by part. Similarly, 'an yaf‘al' is the agent's continuous action on the parts of the patient, like a heater producing heat part by part. The types of action correspond to the types of being acted upon. The author also discusses opposites: being demolished is opposite to being built, etc. Finally, Ibn Sina is cited to justify the preference for the verbal forms 'an yaf‘al' and 'an yanfa‘il' over the nouns 'al-fi‘l' and 'al-inf‘āl', because the nouns may refer to the state after motion, whereas the category is the directed process towards an endpoint.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that action and passion refer to instantaneous occurrences rather than continuous change.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they are continuous processes, as shown by the examples of building a house and heating an object.

Checkpoint
According to al-Farabi as cited in the passage, what does 'أن يفعل' mean?
A) The state after motion ceases
B) Change and moving (التغيير والتحريك)
C) Being changed and being moved
D) The genus of motion

Key Takeaway
The categories of action (an yaf‘al) and passion (an yanfa‘il) are defined as continuous change, with action being the agent's ongoing influence and passion being the patient's ongoing change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes the heater heating part by part continuously. Think of a habit you are trying to change: Are you expecting instant transformation, or are you allowing yourself to be changed (yanfa‘il) gradually, part by part? What would it look like to commit to the continuous process today?
?
Al-Farabi says there is no difference between 'being acted upon' and 'changing and moving'. In what area of your life are you currently being moved by an external agent—perhaps by Allah, by circumstances, or by others? Are you resisting that continuous change, or surrendering to it?
?
The author distinguishes between instantaneous creative acts (like God's creation) and continuous change. What in your life have you tried to achieve through one-time efforts rather than continuous action? Name one thing you will start doing continuously from today.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
4 min
The Species of Motion and the Correspondence of Action and Passion
The author enumerates the correspondence of action and passion types and discusses opposites, then cites Ibn Sina on terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the categories of action and passion correspond to the species of motion, their opposites, and why Ibn Sina preferred the verbal forms.

Core Idea
Every type of passion has a corresponding type of action, and both are opposites in their own categories.

This passage continues from the previous by detailing the correspondence between types of action and passion, enumerating the species of motion, and discussing opposition. Finally, Ibn Sina explains why the verbal forms 'an yaf‘al' and 'an yanfa‘il' are more precise than the nouns 'al-fi‘l' and 'al-inf‘āl'.

Guided Path
1
Examine the correspondence of types
The passage states that for every type of an yanfa‘il, there is a corresponding type of an yaf‘al. Examples are given: takwīn (making) corresponds to takawwun (becoming), binā' (building) to ibtinā' (being built), etc.
2
Understand opposition
The categories also have opposites: being demolished is opposite to being built, and similarly for action categories. Note the symmetry.
3
Study Ibn Sina's clarification
Ibn Sina explains why the verbal forms are used: the nouns can refer to the state after motion, while the verbs denote the ongoing process.
4
Connect to the overall argument
These distinctions reinforce that action and passion are about continuous change, not instantaneous events, which is crucial for the earlier refutation of infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التسلسل المحال وترتب أمور لا نهاية لها مع كونها غير محصورة بين حاصرين : والجواب : إن ذلك إنما يلزم لو كان كل تأثير وإيجاد حتى الإبداعي الذي لا يفتقر إلى زمان من قبيل أن يفعل وكل تأثر وحصول حتى الدفعي من قبيل أن ينفعل وليس كذلك بل إذا كان الفاعل يغير المنفعل من حال إلى حال على الاتصال والاستمرار فحال الفاعل هو أن يفعل وحال المنفعل أن ينفعل حتى فسر الفارابي أن يفعل بالتغيير والتحريك ، وأن ينفعل بالتغير والتحرك . وقال لا فرق بين قولنا ينفعل وبين قولنا يتغير ويتحرك وأنواع هذا الجنس هي أنواع الحركة، ففي الجوهر الكون والفساد، وفي الكم النمو والاضمحلال وفي الكيف الاستحالة، وفي الأين النقلة وحقيقة أن ينفعل هو تصير الجوهر من شيء إلى شيء، وتغيره من أمر إلى أمر ما دام سالكاً بين الأمرين على الاتصال، فالتكون كابتناء البيت قليلا قليلا وشيئاً شيئاً، وجزءاً جزءاً على اتصال إلى أن يحصل البيت. وعلى هذا قياس البواقي وأن يفعل هو أن ينتقل الفاعل باتصال الفعل على النسب التي له أجزاء ما يحدثه فى المنفعل حين ما ينفعل، فالمسخن حين ما يسخن له نسبته إلى جزء جزء من الحرارة التي تحدث فيما يتسخن ينتقل من نسبته إلى جزء من الحرارة إلى نسبته إلى جزء آخر على الاتصال، وأنواعه على عدد أنواع أن ينفعل فان كل تغير وحركة يقابله تغير وتحريك كالتكوين للتكون، والإفساد للفساد وكذا أنواع الأنواع كالبناء للابتناء والهدم للانهدام وعلى هذا قياس التضاد، فكما أن ينهدم مضاد لأن يبتني، وأن يتسخن لأن يتبرد، وكذلك أن يهدم مضاد لأن يبتني ، وأن يسخن لأن يبرد، وعلى هذا قياس البواقي وقال ابن سينا، إنما أؤثر لفظ أن ينفعل وأن يفعل على الأنفعال والفعل لأنهما قد يقالان للحاصل بعد انقطاع الحركة وأنما المقولة ما كان توجها إلى غاية من وضع أو كيف أو غير ذلك مستقر من حيث هو كلك . ولفظ أن ينفعل وأن يفعل مخصوص بذلك.
The impossible infinite regress and the ordering of things without end while they are not enclosed between two encompassing [limits]: The answer: That only follows if every effect and bringing into existence, even the creative which does not require time, were of the type of 'to act' (an yaf‘al), and every being affected and occurrence, even the instantaneous, were of the type of 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il), but it is not so. Rather, when the agent changes the patient from one state to another continuously and perpetually, the state of the agent is that it acts (an yaf‘al) and the state of the patient is that it is acted upon (an yanfa‘il). Thus al-Farabi interpreted 'to act' (an yaf‘al) as change and moving (al-taghyīr wa-l-taḥrīk), and 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il) as being changed and being moved (al-taghayyur wa-l-taḥarruk). And he said: There is no difference between our saying 'yanfa‘il' and our saying 'it changes and moves' (yataghayyar wa-yataḥarrak). And the species of this genus are the species of motion: in substance, generation and corruption; in quantity, growth and decay; in quality, alteration; in place, translocation. The reality of 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il) is the becoming of the substance from something to something, and its change from one state to another as long as it is passing between the two states continuously. Thus, becoming (al-takawwun) is like the building of a house little by little, thing by thing, part by part, continuously until the house is obtained. And the rest are analogous. And 'to act' (an yaf‘al) is that the agent transfers by the continuity of action according to the relations that it has to the parts of what it produces in the patient at the time of being acted upon. Thus the heater, at the time it heats, has a relation to each part of the heat that occurs in what is being heated; it transfers from its relation to one part of heat to its relation to another part continuously. And its [action's] types are according to the number of types of 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il), for every change and motion is confronted by a change and moving, like making (takwīn) for becoming (takawwun), and corrupting (ifsād) for corruption (fasād), and likewise the species of species like building (binā') for being built (ib tinā'), and demolishing (hadm) for being demolished (inhidām). And the rest are analogous. And similarly for opposition: just as being demolished (yanhadim) is opposite to being built (yabtanī), and being heated (yatasakhkhan) to being cooled (yatabarrad), likewise demolishing (yahdim) is opposite to building (yabnī), and heating (yusakhkhin) to cooling (yubarrid). And the rest are analogous. And Ibn Sina said: I only prefer the expression 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il) and 'to act' (an yaf‘al) over [the nouns] 'being acted upon' (al-inf‘āl) and 'act' (al-fi‘l) because these [nouns] may be said for what has obtained after the cessation of motion, whereas the category is what is a direction towards an endpoint of position or quality or other than that, settled insofar as it is such and such. And the expression 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il) and 'to act' (an yaf‘al) is specifically for that.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, why are the verbal forms 'an yaf‘al' and 'an yanfa‘il' preferred over the nouns 'al-fi‘l' and 'al-inf‘āl'?
A) They are simpler
B) They denote the ongoing process rather than the result

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina emphasizes that the verbal forms 'an yaf‘al' and 'an yanfa‘il' specifically denote the process towards an end, unlike the nouns which can refer to the completed state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you evaluate your life by the direction of your actions (an yaf‘al) or by the static outcomes (al-fi‘l)? Which does Ibn Sina say is the true category? How would your daily choices change if you focused on the process rather than the result?
?
When you repent from a sin, do you think of the state after repentance (al-inf‘āl) or the ongoing process of turning back (an yanfa‘il)? Which aligns with the category of passion as direction towards an endpoint? What practical step can you take to live in the verbal mode?
?
In your relationship with Allah, are you more concerned with the completed acts of worship or the ongoing movement towards Him? How can you embody 'an yaf‘al' in your next prayer?
